The remaining limbs, such as the face, neck, throat, tongue, spatula, aid-hand, dorsal vertebrae, pelvis, leg and similar parts of the body are to be cured in various ways; when the arrow does not hit an artery or a nerve; and the arrow is not poisonous.
And I tell you that I have seen such arrows, which indicate by this concerning your cure.
That is because a man had an arrow fall into his tear duct of the eye, at the root of the nose. Extract it from the other side, beneath the ear fat; and he was cured, and no harm came to his eye.
And extract another arrow from the Jew, which fell upon his eyelid beneath the lower lid; and the arrow was already hidden, and I did not obtain more than its tip, which adhered to the wood of it; and the arrow was large among arrows of Turks, with a soft iron, in which there were no ears.
The Jew was therefore cured, and no further harm came to his eye.
And extract another arrow from the throat of a Christian.
And the arrow was Arabian, and it had ears. Cut therefore upon it between two ribs; and already it was immersed in his throat. I became sharp with it, until I extracted it; and the Christian was cured.
And extract another arrow from a man who fell upon his abdomen; and we thought that he would die from it.
After thirty days had passed, and nothing changed in his condition, I cut upon the arrow; and I became sharp upon it, and extracted it; and he was cured, and no further harm came to him.
And I saw a man upon whom an arrow had fallen on his back, and the wound was consolidated over it. After seven years, the arrow emerged at the root of his hip.
And I saw a woman upon whom an arrow had fallen on her abdomen, and the wound was consolidated over it, and the arrow remained within her; and nothing changed in her condition, and the woman did not suffer harm from any of her natural operations.
I saw another man, to whom an arrow fell upon his face; and the wound was sealed, and he remained, not finding him much pain; and similar things many times.
I removed the arrow from the man who was among the King's princes, which had fallen upon his nose in the middle, and it had already turned a little towards the right; and the arrow was completely hidden.
Therefore I was called for his treatment after the fall of the arrow up to three days. I found therefore the wound of the arrow narrowed very much.
I pierced it then with a delicate probe and did not feel it; and himself indeed found the puncture and pain under his right ear. Therefore I hoped that this was the puncture from the end of the arrow.
I plastered therefore the place with a healing ointment containing an attracting and digesting virtue, trusting that the wound would heal, and that the mark of the arrow would appear to me so that I could cut over it; but this did not occur in the place signifying that the arrow had struck.
I remained therefore with the plaster on him for many days; but this did not occur, and the wound healed in the meantime; and he remained weak in extracting it until one day within his nose he felt the arrow. And he told me that.
And he told me that. Therefore I applied on that wound a sharp and corrosive medicine for many days until it opened; and when I pierced it with a probe, I felt the delicate end of the arrow adhering to the wood.
Then I added to the opening of the wound through that sharp medicine until the end of the arrow appeared visibly to us; and it passed by me for about four months with him.
Then, after the wound has been enlarged and it was possible for me to insert forceps into it, and move it, and it did not obey to come out: then I did not cease to refine and contrive over it with methods of ingenuity, until on some day I began with properly made forceps, according to what their form will be at the end of the chapter, until I extracted it. Then, after the wound has been sutured.
And indeed physicians judge that the cartilage of the nose cannot be sutured. I therefore sutured it, and the wound was healed, and the weak one recovered with a healthy integrity; nothing at all harmed him.
And I tell you the quality of extraction of some arrows: so that you will put that rule and meaning on what I have not said.
Because the parts of this art and their distinctions are not understood with a narration; neither does that book comprehend it, and the skilled craftsman compares little to much; and when one is present, on what is absent; and he finds a new operation and a new instrument for descending things foreign to this art, when they occur.
I say therefore that arrows do not come out of the members on which they are fixed, unless according to two modes. Or by being attracted from the place where it entered. Opposite part of another.
And that which comes out from where it entered, or is a projectile going into the fleshly place: and it is drawn, and it goes out.
If it does not obey for exit from the hour at which it falls: then it is necessary that you let it go for some days, until the flesh around it rots: and the attraction of it decreases, and its coming out.
And similarly if it fixes in bone and does not obey for the exit, then let it go also for some days: and apply it every day with pulling and movement, until it comes out.
If it does not obey for the exit after days, then it is necessary that you pierce around the projectile in the same bone from every side with a fine chisel, until the projectile widens. Then pull it and extract it.
If the projectile is already hidden in the body's place and is concealed and hidden from sensation: then search for it with probes. If however you feel it (the tumor), then pull it out with certain instruments that are suitable for its pulling out.
If you cannot pull it (the arrow) on account of the constriction of the wound and the length of the arrow in depth, and there is no bone, nerve or vein there: then fill it (the wound), until the wound expands, so that it becomes possible to extract the arrow.
If it (the tumor) has ears by which it is held: then cut the fixed flesh from all sides with any means you can manage, and the instruments if the cutting of the flesh cannot be made through breaking of the ears and twisting them, until it comes out.
And when you administer the extraction of an arrow in any place, then use twisting with your hand along with forceps to all parts, until it makes it come out: and carefully with utmost subtlety so that the arrow is not broken, and its pulling becomes difficult for you, and its removal.
If you cannot pull it (the arrow) within an hour, then leave it for some days until what is around it putrefies. Then try it again: for the wound will heal itself if the flow of blood opposes you. But if a counteraction occurs to you in the form of bleeding, then administer what we have said in its treatment chapter: and keep your skill away from cutting a vein or nerve or tendon.
And use any means to extract the arrow with ease, peace, or slowness, and firmness as I have told you.
It is necessary that when you pull the arrow, it should be weakened in the same form as at the time of the arrow wound. Because it is more beneficial. If that is not possible for you, then administer what is possible according to figures (arrow shapes).
The arrow that emerges from the opposite part of another, or it already advances something towards the exteriors; or it causes a swelling at the tip of the arrow by sensation from the upper part of the skin nearby, and you see it protruding: therefore cut above it, and let the section be according to the quantity that the forceps can grasp.
Then draw it out: for it becomes easy to come out.
If however it is held in the bone, then turn your hand according to its roundness, until the arrow presses into the bone and enlarges itself; then draw it out.
And if not: let it be for some days; then press it again until it comes out.
If wood is in the arrow, then push it out along with it.
If however the wood has fallen out, and you wish to administer a thrust; then insert a concave instrument into it, so that its concavity enters the tail of the arrow: then push it along with it.
If however the arrow is concave, then push it along with the instrument inserted into it. The arrow is relieved by that [cure].
If the arrow is poisoned, then it is necessary to excavate all the flesh where the venom is; if you are able to do this. Then heal it with what suits for that.
If the arrow falls in the chest, or in the belly, or in the bladder, or in the side: and it is close to that which you touch with your hand, and it is possible for you to cut above it; then cut, and be careful not to cut a vein or a nerve, and pull it out.
Then suture the wound, if you see it, and heal it until it heals up.
And this is the form of forceps with which arrows are pulled out.
Extremities are like the beak of a bird, which have been made as iron: when they take hold of an arrow, they do not release it.
They become various kinds, great and small, and medium-sized, according to the size of the arrow, its fineness, and the width and depth of the wound.
The shape of the projectile is hollowed out.
And the shape of the projectile is deaf.
This is a hollowed-out extremity like a radius, so that its entrance may fit into the concave arrow and be propelled with it.
Chapter on Phlebotomy of Veins.
There are thirty veins that are to be bled whose custom it is to flow in the body.
Among them, there are sixteen in the head: two pulsating veins that are after the ears, called the two Herbs; and two arteries that appear in the two temples; and two veins that are in the two tear ducts of the two eyes, which are called Aspicents; and the prepared vein in the middle of the forehead; and the vein that is at the end of the nose. And two Jugular veins, which are in the neck. And two veins that are in the upper lip of the mouth. And two veins that are in the lower lip. These are the veins that are called Algecherich. And two veins that are under the tongue.
But the veins that are bled in the arms, there are five veins in each arm.
One of them is called Cephalic, and it is from a woody side; the common people call it vein of the head. And black [or dark]. And it is the Median vein, and it is composed of a branch of the Basilica vein, and a branch of the Cephalic vein; the common people call it vein of the body. And Basilica, and it is placed on the domestic side; and it is also called Titillicaris. And the common people call it vein of the abdomen. And Brachial artery, and it is placed on the elbow; it is the one that is cut for bleeding, and it is the one that appears above the thumb in a visible manner.
And Savial artery; it is the vein that is between the ankle and the smallest [finger or toe]; and it has two branches.
And in the leg and foot there are three veins. One of them is on the woody side under the thumb's curvature. And the second one is Saphenous; its place is by the heel on the domestic side. And vein of the ankle; its place is by the heel on the woody side.
And in the other leg there are three veins similar to those.
Chapter on bloodletting from two veins after the ears.
In fact, bloodletting of the two veins that are behind the ears is beneficial for old catarrhs, and migraine, and sores on the head, old sores.
And the quality of bloodletting is as I have said. And it is to shave the head of the sick person; rub the back of his head in the place of the two veins with a rough cloth. Then the sick person should tighten his neck with a ligature until the two veins appear. The places [of incision] are after the ears, in two depressed locations on the head. Therefore, seek them with your finger, and where you feel its pulsation under your finger, mark that spot with the hot iron [cautery].
Then take the phlebotomous knife, and it is called "The Cutter". Then introduce it [the knife] under the vein into the skin, until the phlebotomist reaches the bone. Then move your hand with the vein and skin upwards, and cut the vein along with the skin incision. And let the length of the incision be the size of two fingers joined together, or about that much: and make as much blood flow out as you wish.
Then tie it [the cut vein] with threads, and let it be, until it heals.
And now the incision of them has come to an end, as well as their cauterization, at the beginning of the book.
However, the phlebotomy of the two arteries that are in the two times of bleeding, is an aid to old hemigraine, and sodam prawa, and obtalmia assidua: and to the flow of superfluidity discharged acutely towards the eyes.
And the quality of phlebotomy [of these arteries] is as I have told you. Let the sick person tightly wrap his neck until two veins appear to the senses with clear evidence, and their pulse is shown under your finger. Then mark that spot with hot iron [cautery].
Then move the skin above the vein upwards with your finger as an indicator, and introduce the phlebotomous knife [cutting] from below, and move the vein upwards. And cut it [the vein], as you did with the two other veins: and make blood flow out according to your need.
Then release the restriction of the sick person, and place your finger on the vein at the first hour. Then place a ligature and bandages over it, and bind it tightly with a strong binding; release it when it is healed.
And now the recollection of those (operations), and their incision, and their extraction, begins at the beginning of the book.
Indeed, the benefit of venesection in the forehead after cephalic venesection is for ancient facial diseases, and for hemi-cranium, and redness, and foul ulcers.
And the quality of this venesection, as I have told you. The sick man binds his neck with a bandage until the vein appears. Then take the instrument called "Fossorium".
Place the pointed end that is at the head of the Fossorium on the vein and strike it with a hammer or another tool in the same manner; release the blood to flow according to the quantity you desire. Then release the perforation of the sick person and bind it until it is healed.
And sometimes when the venesection is performed with a wide instrument (such as a vein cutter). However, it is not necessary for the venesection instrument to have a sharp point, unlike the other venesection instruments, but rather one with a wide end. And the venesection should be made across (transversely), because the bone is nearby; and perhaps the venesection instrument may break when it is too sharp.
Indeed, the benefit of the two veins that are in the two tear ducts of the two eyes, is for the eye affected by scabies and redness; swelling of the eye, and facial diseases.
The quality of phlebotomy is that it binds a weak neck with a plaster. Then the blood-letting shall be done: and thou shalt stand over his head.
And the blood-letting shall be done across, less than long, with a small phlebotome, narrower but not too much, for there is no flesh in that place. For if the phlebotome is sharp at the tip, it may break.
Then make him run with as much blood as necessary for you. And place upon them a cloth, and bind them at night, and loosen them.
The veins of the nose for blood-letting are useful for acute fever, severe inflammation of the face, and diseases of the face such as hemigraine and redness that occurs on the face, especially when it is old.
The quality of phlebotomy in this case is to tighten a weak neck. Then hold his nose with your left hand and take a fine, long lancet. Insert it in the middle of the leprosy itself, between that which separates the nose according to its alignment. Since the vein is not visible there and because blood begins to flow immediately.
And it is necessary that your hand with the lancet be slightly curved; and make the blood flow according to your need. Then bind it (the wound) overnight. For it heals quickly.
The advantage of the two phlebotomies by Guidon is for constriction of the breath, and the onset of leprosy, and melancholic illnesses, and impetigo and bad ulcers, and those that are destructive (or corroding), that is, herpes or scabies.
And the quality of their phlebotomy is to tighten a weak person under both necks with a band; and the operator stands over the head of the sick person, and the sick person sits on a chair.
Then the lancet should be about half the length of a wider phlebotomy (lancet). Then draw out an amount of blood that is weighed according to what you see, or according to the necessity that is at hand. Then do the same with another vein.
Then untie the band and tighten both veins with a strap so that the sick person does not suffocate. And let it be for the morning. The wound heals.
Chapter on the phlebotomy of veins (by Albucasis). The advantage of the veins according to Albugasis is because it contributes to alcohol in the mouth and corruption of the gums; and bad ulcers; and cracks in the lips; and bad ulcers, even those that are in the nose, and around it.
And the quality of phlebotomy for them is that you make the sick person sit before you: and bind his neck with a bandage. Then roll up his lip and look at the two veins: one of which is from the right side of the lip, and the second from its left side: and the demonstration of both is by their blackness. And that is true because around them there are small, dark veins. Therefore make an incision on both sides.
If there is doubt about it and you do not know which one it is: then direct your attention to the larger of them, and its more visible.
And similarly do with the two veins that are in the upper lip: and most commonly it is done with this one: and make an incision on the two veins which are in the lower lip.
Chapter of Phlebotomy: The Vein under the Tongue after the Phlebotomy of the Head. But the advantage of the two veins that are under the tongue for phlebotomy is because phlebotomy of the head is for afflictions that are in the throat, and the illness of the larynx, and the illnesses of the mouth.
And the quality of phlebotomizing them is that the sick person should sit between your hands facing the sun: and lengthen his tongue, and look under the tongue on one side to find a vein, and on its other side to find another vein: and the color of each becomes dark.
Therefore make an incision on both sides: and beware that you do not go too deep when phlebotomizing them. For there are arteries under it, and perhaps bleeding may occur from those arteries.
The three veins that are phlebotomized on the arm, those whose phlebotomy is commonly done in all men.
And phlebotomy [is done] according to two modes. Either by pricking with a broad leech (myrtio largo): or by cupping to the subtlety of the vein (oliuando ad subtilitatem decliuis): or cutting with a surgical knife (secando cum phlebotomo cultellari), and it is called Alnesil. And this is its form.
And from these [are] the broad and subtle forms according to the width and tightness of the veins. The form of leeching (phlebotomi myrtini) is broad, as you see. It [concerns] opening concave veins, full, protruding, visible, thick ones, and those that contain thick, turbid blood.
And this leech [is] smaller in width and more subtle for veins that are more superficial.
It [concerns] the leeching of subtle veins that contain fine yellow blood.
The basilica, which is one of the three veins for leeching, provides aid. Because it draws blood from the ailments that are below the throat and neck, following the chest and abdomen; it [is] necessary for the leecher to know this vein, so as to be careful about it, because under it is an artery. Therefore, if there is an error and blood is drawn by pricking with a leech, he will cut the artery, and bleeding will occur. Therefore, it is necessary for the leecher not to prick her with a leech; instead, he should cut with a surgical knife (Alnesil).
If the basilica does not appear in a clear manifestation, then it is necessary to search for another or to exchange it for another; or to seek a branch of it; or to use a leech on its place, instead of an arm (Funem brachii). It is indeed apparent; cut it as Phlebotomus Anesil did, as we said.
If you wish to perform phlebotomy (bloodletting) on it, then it is necessary before the tightening of the arm, that you touch the place, until you recognize the location of the pulse. Then mark the place with hot iron, afterwards the arm, and cut the vein with a concave-shaped knife as Phlebotomus Alnesil did, as we said. And beware that the blow does not fall in the space from the place of the artery.
And when you see, while tightening the bandage, an inflammation at the place which you marked with hot iron, then that inflation is of the artery; avoid it (the blood vessel) therefore.
If you see blood rising at the site of bloodletting, like the urine of a boy, and it is thin, red; then know that it is from the artery's blood. Therefore advance, and place your finger upon it over a long space, afterwards remove your finger. If blood is cut (for it is often cut), then tighten the arm and leave it alone; let the weak avoid movement of it; and beware of fear of it.
And do not move it for a few days until it is healed.
If blood is not cut and it overwhelms you, and medicine is not present at hand for you in the moment; then cut the artery, if it appears to you. For its extremities contract, and blood is cut off.
Or take the bark of the fig tree, and pound it; take one half, and tie it over the place of the vein tightening with a bandage and small stones until another day.
Then if blood is cut, it is good (meaning the treatment was successful). If not (the case): then treat the same person with whom the one who preceded it is remembered, from the place of the dusts causing blood flow abscission; and the bleeding's abscission is not difficult in many dispositions, due to the small size of the wound, and the possibility of tying (ligament) from the arm.
The phlebotomy of the Green Vein is helpful because it draws blood from the upper part of the head and the lower part of the body; because it is composed of a branch of the Basilica vein and the Branch of the Cephalic, as we have said.
It is suitable for the phlebotomist to be aware of this vein, because of fear from its phlebotomy, since there is a nerve beneath it. Therefore, if the phlebotomist adds in piercing and finds a nerve in it, a stupor occurs, and healing becomes difficult, and possibly it is not completely healed.
And this nerve often appears to sensation. If it is hidden in some people and is subtle and not shown, then you must place your phlebotomy where you phlebotomize it, the section with Alnesil, and avoid the nerve with your strength.
If the vein is between two nerves, then cut the vein lengthwise.
On the Cephalic Vein. The phlebotomy of the Cephalic Vein is helpful because it draws blood from the head, and contributes to eye diseases.
It is appropriate in this vein that the phlebotomist be anointed with oil or myrrh before piercing it, since it is safer than all veins because it is not below an artery nor a nerve.
It is fitting for you when you perform venesection (phlebotomy), to ask the barber-surgeon to take only the head of the vein, and to seek a softer place; it does not harm him if no such place is found with the first blow, that he may repeat over it several times with venesection.
Perhaps some men may have a haematoma when not venesection is performed on the first blow, but it does not harm him if such a haematoma occurs somewhere.
On the utility of Venesection and the precautions to be observed. The qualities and circumstances of Venesection and their correction, which you should consider.
First of all, you should know that venesection is not administered unless for the conservation of health and its continuance, and caution against the approach of diseases.
And that venesection should be performed in one of the three veins that are in the elbow, namely the Cephalic, the Radial (Fusca), and the Median (Basilic). And that venesection should be performed at the beginning of spring on fasting days, in summer and autumn after food consumption, when signs of overfullness appear.
And that venesection should be performed on the first or third day after three hours have passed.
Venesection that is administered in diseases has no fixed hour; but when it is necessary and suitable for such a purpose, it may be done at night or day, and at any hour, and at any time.
It is not necessary to perform venesection on boys until they reach the age of fourteen. Venesection should not be performed on pregnant women except from the fourth month up to the seventh. Venesection should not be performed on old men who have already passed sixty years of age.
When anyone wishes to be bled for any reason: it is necessary, that he cleans his intestines before bleeding with a mild purge, if there is much fecal matter retained in them: so that no putrid superfluities from the intestines attract at the time of bleeding towards the principal members.
A patient should not be bled when he is afflicted with toctina (or satiety of nausea), nor when he is vomiting or still under the influence of drunkenness, until that has passed. A person suffering from dropsy should not be bled.
Be careful about bleeding again after a colic attack, vomiting, diarrhea, increase in sexual activity, labor, exercise, vigils, fasting, or any substance that weakens the body's strength from either a material or an animal source.
Then take into account the subtlety of humors before doing this, if the blood is thick with food and drinks, and medications; if it is possible for him to do so.
Then let a bath be entered, if nothing prevents it; or exercise with some exercise, to make the blood subtle.
Place the bleeding of this person on his chest, about the third day, as we said earlier; and try to have him evacuate his chest that day from all bad animal conditions such as anxiety, anger, fear; and all bodily conditions such as labor, punishment, superfluities, and similar ones.
And let him have present the things for which customary use among humans is from their aromatics, scents, incenses, and similar ones, according to his ability.
Then let the person who is bleeding sit on a stool (puluinari), which should be higher than the stool for bleeding; then he shall draw out his arm and rub it with his hand by the bleeding person two or three times. Then tie the ligament with a suture and roll it twice.
And let the constriction be moderate, because an unequal constriction, either by excess in constriction, then it prevents the flow of blood; or in softening, then it also prevents the flow of blood.
Then, after the constriction, the one who performs venesection should rub his hands together until the veins swell up and become visible to the touch.
Then the one who performs venesection cleans the lancet with a little oil, using an ancient method.
Then he should place his index finger of the left hand on top of the vein at the location where he wishes to make the venesection, slightly: so that the vein does not run away and the blow is cushioned. Because the veins are like a string escaping during venesection; and some of them are filled with wind; and when you place the lancet on it, it contracts under the lancet, deceiving the one who performs venesection, and he does not open the vein; and if he opens it, it is constricted: therefore for this reason the one who performs venesection should be firm and careful in all these matters.
Then place the lancet down. If one vein is opened by chance, then it is good. And if not, then try again with another vein in the same place slightly, or above it with haste: if the place is not missed.
If the place is missed or if the weak person faints, then let him go home for a day or two; and do not tie the ligament. Perhaps it will attract an abscess that is hot. Do not enter the bath.
Then repeat the venesection, if needed.
If the opening is small, or blood flows finely: fear lest not the quantity of blood that you wish does not exit; then repeat the venesection in the same hole correctly, and add less in the operation.
And do this with haste before the place is opened, because the opened place is dangerous for many people during small operations.
If therefore you see that the place has already been opened, then do not go over it at all; for nothing can be excused by you in this case. And put something of oil paste on top of it, because it alleviates the flow of blood. And oil is better than this place and than any other oil.
And similarly administer oil paste in every venesection of your own, where the flow of blood is forbidden; and do this as the Tyrian alfarach and Sagzenea when it is placed over the place.
Blood becomes thin and dissolves when it is thick.
If by chance a large abscess occurs at the place of venesection, which often happens especially to those who are not venesectionists except for that occasion, or if the opening of the vein is small; then hurry and put on top of it a sponge soaked in water and salt, warmed slightly: and squeeze it for an hour; because it is dissolved.
And it is necessary that you do this after the blood has left from the vein itself, when its end has been reached, or from another vein.
If something black or green remains at the place after days; it does not hurt it; and if you want, put on top of it something of aloes or myrrh dissolved: or something of mint juice, that is, camphor or similar to it.
And indeed, ulcers and swellings often occur near veins during venesection in the Basilica.
Place your hand upon it (the swelling), then if you find softness under your pressure upon it, then that swelling is evil. Therefore, be careful, lest you place anything on it of what I have said. For perhaps blood from an artery will flow from it.
But make an ointment for it with what is mentioned there (the recipe) to harden the place. Then care for it with the rest of the treatment until it is healed.
And it is necessary, that you draw blood from every man according to the amount of its virtue. And what appears as a red color spreading over the blood (disseminated intravascular coagulation).
For if the blood is black, then let it come out until it turns red; and similarly, if you see it thick, let it go until it becomes thin; and similarly, if it is sharp (hematocrit), let it go until its sharpness disappears.
And it is necessary that the one who is full, strong, and requires bloodletting by one vein, amplify venesection of the vein and be a competent bloodletter; and on the contrary, for the one who is weak, the opposite should hold.
And it is necessary, that you draw it (blood) in the intervals (between veins), and that the opening be narrow; and it is better what is administered in venesection of the vein, that it is a transverse section, not a puncture.
And this method of bloodletting is beneficial in preventing the flow of blood and incising a nerve; and it is more commendable and safer than bloodletting by length and width (venesection).
And who is accustomed to faint during bloodletting, it is necessary that he eat before bloodletting a little bread soaked in water of muskmelon or ginger; or if he is heated, let him be cooled; and draw blood from him in three intervals or four.
If it is of cold constitution, then let him receive before phlebotomy bread soaked in syrup of honey and myrrh, or in syrup sweetened with scented honey and spices, or in scented and fragrant syrup.
If syncope occurs during phlebotomy, and the cause of it is excessive bleeding: then it is necessary that he drinks meat broth, and subtle scented syrup; and let him use aromatics with algalia (a plant or seaweed), and let his chest be swabbed with it; and let him use the remedies mentioned in our previous discussion on syncope due to evacuation.
Whoever wishes to dislocate his arm and let out blood in the second way: it is necessary for him, whose phlebotomy is for great evacuation, and the strength of his constitution is weak, to let out blood gradually by amount of strength day after day.
But whoever wishes to dislocate his arm and let out blood in the second way, and his body is strong: then he shall do it after seven or nine hours from his first phlebotomy.
But whoever wishes to attract blood from his body towards the opposite part where it tends: then let it be dislocated for him on the second or third day.
If there is much blood already heated and sharpened, and it causes a fever: then let the blood come out from him once, and a large quantity be extracted; and the opening should be kept as long as syncope occurs; after which you shall conserve all conditions of phlebotomy; and put your hand on his pulse during the flow of blood, to avoid death in place of syncope. For it often happens when the phlebotomist is ignorant and falls by negligence.
It is not appropriate when you wish to solve the arm, and to release the blood, when already the opening of the vein has been closed, and the exit of blood becomes difficult, that you press strongly upon it and quickly stretch out the cut vein. That is very bad indeed, rather let it be, until it is to be bled again with the lancet.
Either remove with the sharpness of a lancet what has been coagulated from the blood in the opening of the vein and place something dissolved in salt water upon it. And place upon it something from turpentine gum or aloes or sage, and press it gently until the blood comes out.
But if already the vein has become gangrenous, then let it be, and do not cover it up, until the gangrene subsides.
But if necessity calls for the releasing of blood again and there is no excuse, then either bleed that spot or bleed in another vein elsewhere in the arm.
The ligament of the arm is bled in the place of the Fuscus and the Basilicus, when these are not found and hidden because it is composed of these.
And the quality of bleeding from this ligament is that a weak person should insert his hand into warm water until it turns red and the vein appears clearly manifest.
Then, a cord is tied over it with moderately tight tension. Then the lancet is used for second bleeding obliquely gradually, not lengthwise or widthwise; and let the opening be wide, and let your lancing of it be on the junction of the hand slightly.
But if the exit of blood is forbidden, then insert your hand into warm water and let the blood flow into the water until you meet your need.
If you are in the days of summer, then you excuse from the repetition of hands in hot water. Much is placed on the course of blood in hot water during winter.
And this vein's phlebotomy is safer than all other veins because it is not under a pulsating vein nor a nerve.
Indeed, the phlebotomy of the right hand's little finger is beneficial for liver diseases.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is that you tie a leash to the hand with a ligature or with your hand after you insert it into hot water, until the vein swells and becomes very noticeable. Then the phlebotomy of it is made by crossing it (the cut). And if you cut it completely, it will not hurt him at all.
Preserve (it), lest you cut off your hand with the phlebotomy. For under that are the nerves of the fingers exposed from the flesh.
Then repeat the hand to hot water and let the blood run in it. For if you do not repeat it in hot water, the blood coagulates in the vein's opening and prevents its flow.
Since when you remove blood in the required quantity, then place oil and salt on the vein so that it does not coagulate quickly.
And similarly, it is necessary to do this with every constricted branch.
But the aid of this vein's phlebotomy from the left hand is that it is beneficial for spleen diseases; and similarly, do as you did in the second one equally.
The phlebotomy of the saphenous vein is beneficial for diseases that are in the lower part of the body, such as diseases of the womb, retention of menstruation, and weaknesses of the kidneys, and old ulcers of the hips and legs, and similar ones from other diseases.
The quality of phlebotomy is such that the unhealthy one puts his foot into warm water: and he should make friction upon it until the veins swell: and tie above the joint of the foot with a ligature. The vein's location is at the heel, appearing toward the thumb: and they branch out from it on the face of the foot into many branches.
Therefore, phlebotomy should be made in a larger branch of it, or at the heel in its aggregation: because it is better and safer.
If phlebotomy is made on the face of the foot, then beware of the nerves that are under it on the surface of the foot.
And place your phlebotomy instrument perpendicularly, as if you wish to cut it: and the phlebotomist is Nesil.
If the outlet of blood is forbidden, then immerse his foot in warm water: and let the blood flow into it until it is evacuated.
If the phlebotomist errs in the first vein, then repeat with phlebotomy to the upper part slightly, for it is safe, and do not fear any harm from it, as long as you beware of the nerves, as we have said.
And similarly, with the saphenous vein of the other foot equally.
The location of the sciatic vein, as we have said, is at the heel from the forest side.
And the benefit of phlebotomy is for pain in the ankle, when it is due to hot blood.
The quality of phlebotomy is such that the unhealthy one enters the bath and hurries: and bind the leg from the ankle to above the heel with a thin, long bandage, for it does not appear unless for this reason.
When it appears, then perform phlebotomy as possible for you, either according to the transverse method: and it is better, or make an incision into her, or cut her off with a section. The place of this is safer, and it is very hidden in many people.

Then unravel the stricture, and place skin over the site of phlebotomy, and bind the site. It is quickly healed.
On ventosation and the quality of its use.
They are made from horns, and from brass, and from glass.
And ventosation is done according to two modes. With incision and bloodletting: and there is another ventosation without incision.
And this ventosation which is without incision, is according to two modes. Or it is with fire. Or it is without fire.
And the places of the body where ventosation with incision and bloodletting are administered are fourteen. One of them is Ventosation Alnocrati, and it is the last part of the head: and Alkehel, and it is in the middle of the neck: and ventosations Alhagdahim, and they are two plates of the neck from both sides at once: and ventosations of the brain, and it is under the jaw on the lower part of the mouth: and ventosations of the two spatulas: and ventosation Alachosos above the end of the tail: and ventosations of the two fists, and they are in the middle of two arms: and ventosations of the two legs: and ventosations of the two calves.
And Ventosation does not draw blood from you from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and therefore its power does not fall out of them, as is the case with its failure due to venesection. It is not necessary that Ventosation be administered with fire or without it for any of the illnesses caused by congestion, until the entire body has been evacuated.
If necessity requires Ventosation due to illness or custom, administer it at the beginning of the month, at its end, and in the middle, and at any time whatsoever.
It is because there are men whose blood multiplies in them until it requires the extraction of it through Ventosation, who find a headache and swelling in their heads.
And there are those who find congestion and redness in their faces, in their heads, and in their necks. And there are those who find itching in their faces and foreheads, and darkness, and dullness in their eyes.
And there are those who have a pain at the place of their Ventosation. And there is one whose laughter is increased. And there are those who find the taste of blood in their mouths: or their tongue is scraped and the blood is sponged off. And there is one whose sleep is increased. And there are those who see blood and redness in their dreams, and the dead, and wounds, and things similar to them.
When we see something from these [symptoms], and especially if it is during the third week of the month: we order Ventosation to be performed, after two or three hours have passed since that day.
The benefit of Ventosation for the neck is that it alleviates headache and that what is released goes to the eyes. It is necessary that it [the lesion] should be after evacuation of the entire body.
This [method] is called ventosa in the place of cephalic phlebotomy.
And it must not be used by one who has a cold temperament or where there is a catarrh. For it brings great harm to him.
And for that reason, it should not be used by the elderly, and in one whose head is afflicted with cold diseases.
He who becomes persistent over it generates oblivion upon himself.
And for that reason, you should instruct the ventosa operator to place his hand with the cupping glass slightly lower, fearing the generation of oblivion.
Cupping (ventosatio or alkehel) is in the place of phlebotomy Fusca, and Basilica phlebotomy, and for that reason it brings shortness of breath, constriction of the windpipe, shortness of breath due to instrument fracture, coughing, and repletion.
And it must be pushed slightly the cupping glass (alkehel), because if placed lower, it generates weakness in the heart and the stomach.
Cupping (alhagdahim) brings pain to accidents of the head, migraine, and optic neuritis, perforation, and pain in the roots of teeth; it is in the place of basilica phlebotomy.
And you should instruct the ventosa operator not to press his hand with scarification, lest he cut an artery and cause bleeding.
Cupping under the chin brings drunkenness to the mouth, corruption of gums, and similar problems that are in the mouth; it takes the place of another type of phlebotomy, which is located on the two lips.
Cupping with a spatula brings alchafacham, which occurs from repletion and heat.
Internal cauterization of two fires assists the phlebotomy of three veins, Basilica, Fusca, and Cephalica: since it draws blood from all those fine veins that are in the flesh; and those fine veins draw blood from other larger ones until the attraction reaches the three veins.
It is necessary that you instruct the cauterizer not to proceed too much in scarification: because there is flesh exposed, and beneath it are veins and nerves.
The ventose which is applied to the anus assists hemorrhoids of the anus and lower ulcers.
It is necessary that you instruct the cauterizer that the ventose should be large and of iron: because the place needs strong suction, and perhaps small ventoses may break; and it should be scarified with a deep scarification.
The ventoses of the legs diminish with an obvious reduction: because they draw blood from the entire body, and provide old pains in the kidneys, womb, and bladder; provoke menstruation, and confer inflammation and coldness; and stand in place of the phlebotomy of two saphenas and alhorcobeni.
However, they weaken the body greatly, and cause fainting in many men.
And the ventose of alhorcobeni assists closely the assistance of the ventoses of two legs.
The quality of the position of the ventoses is that you should place the ventose first in an empty place, and suck with moderate suction; and do not prolong the position of the ventoses, but place them quickly, and remove them quickly, so that humors come to the site at equal arrival; and do not cease to repeat this and continue until you see the place already redden and inflame, and the redness appears as blood; then scarify, and gradually repeat suction. Then consider in the arrangement of bodies.
For whoever is from soft-fleshed people, rare pores, it is necessary to scarify him once, not more: so that the place does not ulcerate.
And command to the Wind-healer (anesthetist), that he should enlarge the scarification and not too deeply: and temper the sucking in ease, speed, and gentle motion.
If there is swelling in blood, then it is necessary to scarify him twice. In the first instance, a way is opened for subtle blood flow and its liquefaction: and in the second instance, to the thorough examination of the extraction of coagulated blood.
If indeed the blood is extremely foul, then let there be a third scarification: so that it follows the last.
And finally, when we want to draw out blood little by little or less, one scarification is sufficient for us. If we want the extraction of blood many times, let there be multiple scarifications.
And if we measure that the blood is thick: then it is necessary to scarify with deep scarifications: and the end equal in scarifications, is the depth of the skin.
What is that which should be administered from the oils at the position of the Wind-healers (anesthetists), and what the anesthetist should avoid.
In the thick-skinned person, hard, dark, and whose pores are narrow, it is necessary to anoint with oils of opening (anesthetics), softening, resolving: if it is in the summertime, as aloe oil, or olive oil, or oil from sweet almonds, or oil from pumpkin seeds.
And if it is in the wintertime, then narcissus oil, or lily oil, or chamomile oil, or soap (sabun): and similar to these.
If the superfluity is thick and cold, then use bitter almond oil, or oil from balsam or white oil, or oil of anise; and similar ones.
If the wound is large and filled with putrid flesh, then it is necessary to prohibit oil and clean the places of the wounds after lancing them with rose water, or cold water, or salt water, or cucumber water, or portulaca water; and similar ones.
He whose blood is much moisture, clean the wounds with vinegar, or with water of myrtle, and sumac; and similar ones.
He whose superfluities are thick, clean the wounds with old wine, or with juniper water, or anise decoction, or chamomile; and similar ones.
It is necessary to avoid lancing in the bath or near the bath, rather it should be administered after leaving the bath for an hour, or two hours.
And it is not advisable that anyone sleeps after lancing.

Chapter on ventositation without scarification. Ventosations which are without scarification are those that are placed on the liver, spleen, foot-affliction, and on the abdomen, navel, places of the kidneys, and the ankle-blister: because these members cannot bear scarification upon them.
Nor is anything sought by these, except attraction of blood from one member to another; such as placing a ventosation on the breasts in the illness of nosebleed. And we use them to dissolve the cold and unwelcome ventosity in one member by another; such as placing a ventosation on the abdomen and navel. For it makes the member rarefied, and warms it, and removes pain therefore, because it dissolves that ventosity.
Also it is placed on the kidneys, when there occurs abscess or stone. By its virtue perhaps it opens an abscess, or expels a stone from its place.
And similarly it does when placed on the liver and spleen for the ventosity to be drained from either of them.
These are administered empty with incision only. And at times they are administered with fire.
And at times they are filled with warm water for diseases of pleurisy: and that is to fill the ventose bag and it should be large, hot water alone, or water in which boiled herbs suitable have been put.
Â§ Shape of a ventose administered with fire.
The width of its opening should be two fingers apart, as we have made it; and the depth should be half a palm; and on the side of it, in a place approximately halfway along its length, there should be a small hole, according to the size that can enter it with an awl; it should be made of brass or citronwood, smooth, equal-edged blades, cleansed; so as not to harm the member at its position above it; and in its middle, a transverse rod of brass or iron, where the candle is placed with fire.
And now this ventose is made large, larger than we have described, but slightly smaller than the one mentioned earlier, according to the diseases and age of the person who uses it.
For ventoses of children, and of those weakened, are different from the ventoses of men, and of fat bodies.
The quality of placing this ventose with fire on a member is that you should light a linen cloth or a small candle from wax; and place it on the middle transverse rod that is in the middle of the ventose; so that the fire is burned approximately below the lower part of the ventose, to prevent burning of the weak body.
Then it should be placed on the member; and a finger should be over the hole that I have said, until we hold the ventose; while we need it, we remove the finger, and steam will come out through the hole, and the ventose is immediately released.
Then, linen (you) heat according to its manner, and if necessary, repeat it.
In pneumothorax, which is administered in the sickness of pleurisy, there is no bladder with a cross or hole: and it is not administered unless because it is filled with water, and is placed only on the body part.
And this is its form.
And this pneumothorax, the larger it is, in order to hold much water, is better.
Â§ On Suspension of Sugar-water.
Sangue-sugars are not administered in many cases, unless in those members where the position of ventoses is not possible due to their smallness, such as lips, gums, and similar parts. Or in a member denuded of flesh, such as fingers, nose, and similar parts.
And the quality of use is that you come from Sangue-sugars which are in sweet waters, free from decay.
Then, they should be let go every day and night into sweet water until they are hungry, and there remains nothing left in their stomachs. Then the body is bled first with phlebotomy, and with ventoses. Then cleanse the ill member until it turns red. Afterwards, they are placed on top of it.
When they are filled and fall, and it is possible to suck at the place with a ventose, then it is more beneficial for healing; and if not, then cleanse the place with vinegar. Then, with much water, rub and squeeze.
If the flow of blood is prolonged after the loss of Sangue-sugars, and it is exuding; then immerse a linen cloth in cold water, and place it on top until the exuding ceases.
If blood is multiplied, then pulverize over it zeji ground or gall-stones, and similar ones from poppy seeds: until the blood is cut off.
Or pulverized almonds (fabarum) without their shells are placed on the site and left until the beans stick to the place, for blood is cut off.
And it is necessary that if a repetition of Sanguisugarum (a sugar extract from the sap of the blood-leaf plant) is needed, it should be changed when possible.
But if Sanguisuga (leeches) are forbidden from being hung up, then clean the site with fresh blood. Or insert an aculeus (a pointed instrument or needle) into the sites until something comes out of the blood. Then place them, for they themselves will stick immediately when they sense something from the blood.
Whenever you want them to fall (off), pulverize something from ground aloes, salt or ashes over them, and they will fall immediately.
This Chapter is also frequently necessary in the art of Medicine and it is restoration for fractures and dislocations occurring in bones.
Know ye, O sons, that foolish doctors and the common people boast about this Chapter, and those who have never looked at an ancient book or read a letter from it.
And for this reason, this method has been deprived of learned men in our land. And I have never used it in a good way, nor have I acquired that from it except for the length of reading ancient books and my diligence in understanding them until I extracted the knowledge of that method from them.
Then I attach myself to experiment and study throughout my life.
And now I have described for you the entire content of that chapter containing my knowledge; and above what my experiment exceeded, after abbreviating it for you, and freeing it from occupation with elongation, and abbreviating it to its end of abbreviation, and showing it at its end of declination: and I have formed in it many forms of instruments that are administered within it, since it is due to the addition of declination, as I did in the two preceding chapters.
Chapter II.
It is necessary that we say in the prologue of this chapter the main points and headings, to which the craftsman must be directed first regarding their truth: namely, that you and those who are ready should know or learn this noble art from each other.
Therefore, I say that when a fracture or dislocation or twist or accident happens to someone, then it is necessary to rush at first to bleed him and free him, or both simultaneously: unless something prevents it, such as weakness of strength; or he who it has happened to is a child or an old man decrepitus; or it is a time of intense heat or intense cold.
Then he should be content with his food, cold vegetables, beans and cabbage: and forbid wine, coarse meats, and to be filled from foods and all food that fills the veins with blood. Until when you are secure from a hot apoplectic attack, and do not fear the flow of matter to the limb: then let the sick person return to his own regimen first with which his custom has been accustomed.
When therefore the broken bone begins to heal, then it is necessary that the sick person be fed with nourishing foods in abundance, thick, heavy, containing viscosity: such as food made from cooked wheat with water, rice, and heads, and feet, and innards of cows, and eggs, and fresh fish; and thick wine, and similar things. When this regimen is followed, the healing of the fracture happens more quickly and better.
And know that broken bones, when they are in men who are in the first old age and decrepit, it is not possible for them to be joined and consolidated according to their nature always, because of the drying up of their limbs; and they are consolidated and joined only at the end of the softness, such as the bones of small boys.
However, nature connects something similar to a glue on every part of the broken bone, in which there is thickness, by which it adheres and tightens it until they are consolidated to each other, and bound together until it comes to the end of strength and firmness, as it was at first, until it does not prohibit something from its operations; and for this reason, it is necessary that you give nourishment to the sick with foods that have thickness, viscosity, and heaviness, as we said.
And know that the kinds of fractures are various, according to the diversity of the limbs; because the fracture of the leg is different from the fracture of the bone of the head; and the fracture of the bone of the chest is different from the fracture of the bone of the back; and all other limbs are different from each other in this way; and I come with a reminder of every kind of fracture exposed in its own chapter distinguished from others. The species of bone fracture are also diversified within themselves.
Because the fracture is arched, stars also happen in it. And sometimes the fracture is along the length of the bone; and there are fractures called stars, that is, points, and additions protruding, like in a reed when it is cut with a knife for writing. And not protruding; and there is the fracture with wound and dislocation at the head; and there is the small crack fracture.
And every kind has a skill, or its own tool for restoration, according to what its memory comes up, distinguished in its places.
And from that which the bone fracture is known, there is its twist and its protrusion; and its appearance to the sense and its sound when you compress it with your hand.
When therefore there is no twist appearing nor sound under your touch when you touch the bone with movement, nor does a weak great pain appear; then there is no fracture there, indeed it is possible that there is a twist or small crack; therefore do not move it with your hand and compression at all, but put medicines over it whose memory comes up after some time; then tie a string around it with a fine ligation.
And know that the place becomes arched and is split into two, but stars appear in it; however, all parts should be removed from each other; then you must begin to start at your hour for its correction, aligning it before a hot abscess appears on it.
If however a hot abscess appears on it, then leave it for some days until the abscess subsides. Then align it according to whatever method possible with gentleness and skill.
And know that the restoration and its equality is easier than the bone, where complications occur.
Then tie it up, according to what comes to your recollection of it.
If there are complications in the bone, then an extension of this broken member is necessary from both sides, whether it be a hand or foot; or with your hand if the member is small; or with two ropes, or with a rope and your hand.
And let your position be such when you place the member on a level surface according to its natural shape, until the parts of the broken member are extended; then try to bring back all additions with every effort and in whatever way possible at their places; and beware lest, by your operation, pain or injury occurs, nor harm; and try with your strength, to apply one of two bones together with its companion, according to better shapes.
And it is necessary that you probe it at that time with your hand. If therefore you see anything different there, correct it and make it equal by the amount of your strength.
And beware of violent stretching and strong compressions, as many foolish people do. For they often cause an abscess to occur due to their operation or a contraction in the member; as we have seen this happens many times from their operations.
Then attach after the equality and suitable operation, and compression, relaxation, and peace of that member; and warn the weak person about it. And make him take care not to move it during his waking hours and sleep, during its rotation, its stirring, and during its exertions, and all his movements with the last of his strength. And so that it may be avoided, let there not be a position of a limb with which follows pain. Whenever there is pain or injury while in the position of a limb, then it must be moved to another position in which no pain is felt.
And see that its position is equal and straight, so that there will not be a twisting in the limb when it is restored.
The quality of the constriction of the broken member, as I have told.
Know that fractured members are differentiated according to their size and form.
Those that are small, such as the arm, finger, fleshy part of the ear, and similar to these, then it is necessary that the bandage be made of soft, fine fabric. Those that are large, such as the hip and leg, it is necessary that there be a wide, firm bandage around them.
Because a broad ligament binds a large member and compresses it from all sides with equal constriction, so that no spot remains inside of it.
Then begin after your evacuation to place a suitable poultice on it at the soft spot of the fracture itself. Then begin to wrap the bandage around the location of the fracture itself with three or four wrappings, according to what the member deserves; and bind your hand slightly with the bandage.
Then go to the upper part from the location of the fracture and bind it less than your previous constriction, with which you bound the fractured place. Then extend yourself and the bandage slightly away from the location of the fracture and gradually soften the constriction until you gather much from a healthy place.
Then take another poultice, and wrap it again over the fracture with bandages. Advance with subsidence towards the lower part from the fracture. And let your operation be in the constriction of subsidence, and softening it, as we said in the first subsidence above.
Then place between the subsidences from soft sponge or cloth, which corresponds to the crookedness of the fracture, if there is crookedness in it; and if not, do not put anything in it.
Then subside upon another piece of meat above it. Then place equal stars (stitches) well made immediately on top of these subsidences, if there is no swelling or warm abscess in the area.
If there is swelling or abscess in it; then place upon it what sits on the abscess and remove the swelling; and leave it for some days. Then bind over these with stitches.
Let the stitches be half of a thick reed, smooth and well formed; or let them be from wood where sieves are made, which are made of pine or oak, or calingi: and let the width of each stitch be according to this shape and this same figure.
However, it is necessary that the stitch which is placed upon the swollen part be slightly thicker and wider than the other stitches; and the length of the stitch should be according to what is suitable for the limb in size and smallness.
Then bind over the stitches with another piece of linen, following the first constriction. Then bind over with threads well, as we said about the constriction; and let your constriction be over the fracture place more; and the farther it extends from the fracture, the less the constriction should be.
It is necessary that the threads be of medium thickness and fineness, and they should be from soft linen.
If threads are thick, as I have seen in the operation of stupid people putting their threads twisted from the cord of flax, it is a great mistake: because the constriction falls out with them beyond its quality.
Fine threads do not agree again: since you will not obtain what you want through the constriction with them.
It is not necessary that there be less than a finger between one star and another.
If the sick person is injured by the points of the stars after the constriction in healthy places: then place beneath them from soft wool or carmine-coated wool, until nothing is injured from that.
When there is a break with a wound, and there is a rupture on the skin, I shall come for its reminder in Chapter 2 under special circumstances.
And know that not every broken limb should be strung with stars from the first day. For since a limb is large when it is large, you should not place stars on top of it unless after five days, or seven, or more, according to your safety from the coming of a hot apoplectic fit.
This is the method of ointments, which the ancients used to place on a fracture and dislocation.
Common Ointment Method, restoring a fracture, suitable for many temperaments, especially women and children, since it is from that which heat does not conquer nor cold.
And you should receive from the flour mill dust, and it is the marrow of wheat that hangs on the walls of the mill near the movement of the millstone: and make it as such, except that you sieve it with egg white: and place the mixture not thick, nor thin. Then use it.
Another Ointment Method, restoring a fracture, and dislocation, and twisting. Prepare a paste with almess, laudanum, acacia, enula, and mugath. Grind all of them together with ten parts of myrrh, five parts of aloes, and twenty parts of althea. Strain the mixture and sift it. Mix it with water of althea, or with sheep's milk whey if the patient's complexion is heated. Use this paste as a plaster. For it is of good composition, restores broken bones quickly, and is beneficial for many people.
Another method of applying a plaster, which also helps with fractures and dislocations. Prepare mugath, mess, white althea, all ground together with ten parts of myrrh, five parts of aloes, six parts of acacia, twenty parts of Armenian clay, and grind them thoroughly, sift them, and make into a paste with water or sheep's milk whey. Administer it.
Another method of applying a plaster for joints that are dislocated and bones separated from their places: it soothes the pain, heals the broken bone, and stops the cracking. Take boiled wool, immerse it in vinegar and heated oil, and place it on the affected area.
In this plaster, there is no power of restoration, but it is good for the soothing of a hot abscess and for expelling specific pains.
Another method of applying a plaster that restores broken bones. Take the leaves of fig tree and wild papaver, grind them all, and make a plaster from both.
Another method of applying a short-term plaster for the restoration of broken bones and when you want to dissolve the remnants of an abscess. Prepare the root of althea, chamomile, marsh marigold flowers, and the flour of herbs, all in equal parts. Grind them all and make into a paste with wine if the affected area is not heated.
If it is heated, then prepare it with water of coriander steeped in water, or use it.
Another stronger method for resolving an abscess, which is stronger than the first, when a hard abscess occurs during the healing of a limb. Take the root of althea, linseed seed, balustia, lentil, samsuc, violets, camomile and mix them all together, grind each part into one, then prepare it with water of willow or sweet water, or wine. Apply the whole thing according to the heat of the limb, and the calmness of its warmth.
Hippocrates however did not say in his book that it should be placed on a broken limb during its healing, except for wax made from tallow and oil; not anything else; and he said that it should be of medium thickness between coarseness and fineness.
Galen however considered it appropriate to place on a broken limb during its healing substances that have the property of extracting heat, such as myrrh, aloes and frankincense and similar substances from what we have mentioned.
Method of plaster that helps with injury and pain. Take mugath, chickpeas, human hair cuttings or bird feathers, althea, and salt in equal parts: grind them, sift them, and prepare plaster from it.
The quantity that should remain as ligature is such that you consider whether pain does not occur, nor itching, nor the bone moves from its place; then do not dissolve it for many days. If there is intense itching, restless pain, or swelling at the site; then hurry and dissolve it within an hour, remove the plaster from it; and take a soft cloth or sea sponge, moistened with warm water and clean the place with it until the itching subsides and the pain settles.
If there is intense itching, restless pain, or swelling at the site; then hurry and dissolve it within an hour, remove the plaster from it; and take a soft cloth or sea sponge, moistened with warm water and clean the place with it until the itching subsides and the pain settles. Then let the member rest for one hour. Then place upon it a woolen cloth soaked in vinegar and oil, or rose oil: and bind over it at night until you are certain of the absence of the hot inflammation, and let the swelling of the member subside, and remove the pain from it.
Then repeatedly apply it to a narrow bandage and small plaster: and do not bind it with your first constriction, and with fine ones until it is healed.
If you see that the abscess, redness, pain, swelling, and all other signs have been completely removed: then apply the plaster and bandage equally as you did at first.
If nothing of the aforementioned occurs in the member: then do not loosen it until three days, or four, or five, or seven. And when it is released after twenty days, according to what you see in the solution of the member, as we have said: until such time as the fracture adheres and coagulation of flesh over it: add also more to your bandage than your first bandage: and add also more nourishment for the weak, according to the memory of its previous condition.
If you see that the location of the fracture is already drying out and becoming too thin: then know that nourishment must not reach it. Therefore pour warm water on it at every place where you loosen it: and let it be for all three days, and alleviate the bandage slightly.
Therefore by this operation nourishment flows to the member, and it heals quickly.
However what stupid restorers do for a fracture of the member instead of restoration as it should be: when it is not restored first according to necessity, and restored according to twists: it is an error from their operation, and great fear: and if it were correct, the ancients would have said that in their books, and they would have operated with it.
And I have not seen any trace of theirs there at all, and it is better that it is not done.
On the making of an ulcer in the head.
The types of fractures are various, and their shapes are many; and their causes are separate.
For from a fracture is that which occurs from a blow with a sword; and the blow removes the bone completely as far as it reaches to the pia mater, which is under the bone, just as an axe does in wood; and because of this, this type of fracture is called Axial.
The knife has cut something of a bone and has cut its face, but it does not pierce through the incision to the end of it; and this type of fracture is called Kalaman, that is, Absolute Lever.
And it is a wound of two types of fractures, either large or small.
And from the fracture is that which is a crush or rubbing; and its cause is striking with a stone, or falling upon a stone, or similar things.
And this fracture is also piercing, which approaches the cloth that is under the bone; or it is on the face of the bone; and it is a wound of these two types of fractures, either small or large.
And from the fracture is that which is hidden within the bone in the subtlety of hair; and it is a small crack, and because of this it is called this type of fracture Capillary.
And from them is a fracture which occurs from falling or striking with a stone and similar things, which cause the lamina of the bone to enter into the interior; and it forms a hollow: as happens with cauldrons of brass, when they are struck and part of them enters into the interior.
And it often occurs in the moist skull bones, such as those of children.
And all these types of fractures are separated by sawing, and I will go with the reminder of the entire thing to its place.
And we recognize all these types of fractures by examination on it and investigation of them with probes, and removal of the rotten flesh that is over them.
But the Capillary type is recognized by this: it is exposed on the bone and scraped off, and rubbed upon it with a hot iron; for the fracture appears black.
In treating a fracture, consider first the condition of the sick man; then if you see from his conditions that it indicates fear clearly, as vomiting cholera, extension, loss of intellect, abscission of voice, fainting, acute fever, discharge of eyes, redness of them and similar things in many dispositions, without a doubt he will not be cured; and if you see conditions that do not frighten you and you hope for salvation; then begin the treatment.
If a wounded person comes to you first with a wound, and it is during the days of winter, then it is necessary that you should study in the removal of the bone before the fourteenth day according to every arrangement.
If it is during the days of summer, then you should hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before what is beneath the bone decays from fat and such things as we have mentioned happen.
If the fracture of the bone has already reached the fat touching the brain and is with fragments and traction, then you should cut the fragmented and smoothed part according to what I have told you.
And it is necessary that you shave the head of the wounded and pierce through the bone according to whatever method is possible for you: and according to the shape of the wound and what is light on what is weak.
If there occurs to you during the piercing of the bone a flow of blood or a hot abscess, then apply what is necessary to it. And it is necessary that you fill the place with soaked cloths in wine and rose oil: and let the wound rest until the abscess settles down and you are secure from the flow of blood. Then begin in the healing of the bone, and removal of it.
And this is done according to one of two methods.
One method is to cut the bone with a knife with a narrow blade: and this is its form.
Then administer another knife wider than the first: and this is its form.
Then administer yet another knife wider still.
And the second method is that you have a number of knives of various sizes, some of which are wider than others and some of which are shorter than others: and they should be at the end points of their sharpening, and made of iron or hardened: and use them with ease in striking the knife, so as not to shake the head and hurt it.
If the bone is hard and tough: then it is necessary that you perforate around its circumference before you administer Incisoria, with Terebris which are called Terebra not penetrating deep: and they are not named thus unless because they do not penetrate beyond the bone marrow to what lies after it; therefore, because the tip of the Terebra is rounded above that which is under its head sharp, similar to an edge, and a small circle prevents submerging, and penetrating through the thickness of the bone.
It is necessary that you take a great number of these Terebris, one of which should correspond to the thickness of the bone; until the present has all the Terebrum of the skull: whose sharp tip's quantity in length and breadth should be according to the thickness of that skull.
And this is the form of three types of Terebris or Trepans.
The quality of perforating around the bone that is broken, is: that you place the Terebra on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated. Then exchange the Terebra to another place and place what is between every hole of a size equal to the thickness of a finger or about it.
Then cut with Incisoria what is between every hole from the bone: and do it more subtle than possible over that from the end of delicacy, as we said, until you have removed the bone, either by your hand or with some other tools which you have prepared for this; such as tenalias and delicate forpices.
And it is necessary that you be careful with all caution, that the Terebra or Incisoria touches nothing from silk.
When you perforate the bone by going around and it is separated from silk, if it was continuous with it, and you remove it, it is necessary that you shave, and equalize the remaining part in the bone with another similar Incisoria. It is necessary that it should be thin and subtle more than other incisors.
If something remains from small bones and cartilage, then take it with ease, with what is near you among your tools. Then care for the wound after that with linen, and the ointments that I mentioned.
Another method of operation is easy, completely distant from fear; which Galen said and praised greatly; and this is his statement. You must first begin to expose a piece of bone from the place where the fracture is more violent and clear; until when you have exposed that part and placed under it the tip of this Convex Incisor, which has this form.
The convex part does not cut anything; and the sharp part of it extends on both sides in length, as you see; so that the convex part may be attached to the splint, with its face incidentally sharp on the bone.
Then strike the Convex Incisor on one side with a small hammer until you cut all the bone with ease, as it is rotated; and you will be safe from the cloth that does not fall into the place you fear completely.
And if the doctor were not foolish among men, and more ignorant of this, nor asleep, then if something remained attached to the bone from the cloth in some places, he would cut it off with the tip of the Convex Incisor and free it with ease. It is freed from it without harm and without fear.
If the bone fracture does not penetrate to the cloth and what is cut away from the bone is its face, and roughness and subtle cartilage remains in it; then it is necessary that you remove the roughness, and dig out the cartilages with finer rasors that you have prepared of various sizes, so that it will be possible for you to administer them in every place more suitable and correct according to where the operation leads you, and the fractured bone, and its figure. It is necessary that you administer [the procedure] according to your reasoning, firstly cutting the bone more widely with those broader scalpels. Then use a finer instrument after that.
It is necessary that you administer [the procedure] according to your reasoning, firstly cutting the bone more widely with those broader scalpels. Then use a finer instrument after that. Keep doing that continuously until you reach administering it more thinly and more finely with them all.
In each of the remaining small cracks and subtle fractures, it is necessary that you administer care according to what leads to its correction.
And there is a thing that does not hide upon every one who has any study in this art and it stands upon what we have written and shown in large fractures.
If however a piece of flesh is detected while you are cutting the bone: then it is necessary that you take linen cloth according to the size of the wound and immerse it in rose oil and place it over the wound opening. Then take another cloth doubled or tripled and immerse it in wine and rose oil and place it on top of the first one: and do that as easily as possible over it so that it is not too heavy.
Then administer a wide bandage over it and do not tie it except according to the amount that holds the wound only: and leave it with this care for one day or two days until you are certain about hot swelling. Then dissolve it and administer afterwards medications with which excessive dryness is necessary: such as the roots of garlic, flour of herbs, powder of frankincense, Aristolochia, and similar ones: and it is so that you make a powder from these medications and sprinkle it over the wound like dry ones.
And administer universally in that all medication with a property to cleanse and not bite. Be careful according to your virtue, so that the wound may be clean during your treatment: neither should there be dirt from ointment, nor dirtiness.
Be careful according to your virtue, so that the wound may be clean during your treatment: neither should there be dirt from ointment, nor dirtiness. Do not let pus gather at all on it, because when pus gathers upon the brain tissue, it corrupts it and putrefies it, and a great evil happens after that to the sick person: and already has happened on the skull, when the bone is cleaned by him, especially when the care of it is neglected, blackness appears on its surface.
Therefore, if the afflictions that have been mentioned occur to the sick person, know that it is certainly dangerous.
And if the blackness does not occur, except from the medicine that is put upon him, and it is in the virtue of that medicine to make the blackness: then you should receive a part from honey, three parts from rose oil, and wash with them cloth. Then place it upon the skull: afterwards take care of it with suitable remedies for healing, until it heals.
Chapter on the restoration of a broken nose.
Know that the nose is not broken except for two of its upper parts at once, or one of them: because it has two bones, since below it is cartilaginous and does not break, nor does it happen to it except by abrasion, curvature, and deviation.
If one of its two parts is broken, then you should insert a small finger into the nose's opening and align the fracture with your index finger and thumb on the outside until the nose returns to its natural shape: and do this skillfully with ease. And be careful that pain does not occur upon the sick person due to your operation.
