If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose, and it is not reached by the finger: then it is necessary to equalize the end of the radius, where there is a small swelling.
If the fracture occurs in two parts, then do as follows: and approach with its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if it is possible. And if not, then after the seventh or tenth day, at rest, let the aposteme be made with warm poultices.
Then insert a piece of linen cloth into the end of the nose, if the fracture is in one part: or insert two pieces of linen cloth if the fracture is in two parts: and let there be in the cloth a swelling of such size that it fills the nostril.
And some old restorers said that they should be soaked in butter and changed every day. But this does not seem to me, on the contrary, it is necessary that the linen cloth should be soaked in egg white, made with flour mill. Then release the linen cloth until the bone hardens and the cartilage thickens.
And sometimes a feather of a goose is inserted into the nose instead of the linen cloth, after it has been covered with soft fabric: and this happens so that the feather tightly holds the fracture of the nose, and prevents the weak from being hindered in breathing.
And this is not necessary. If you wish, do it: and if you wish, do with linen cloth.
If the nose again becomes infected while performing an aposteme with warm poultices, plaster the nose with pitch or with copper pledget soaked in rose oil or a little with tannin.
But if there is no infection of the warm poultice, then it is necessary to plaster it with wheat flour and frankincense powder mixed with egg white. Then place a soft cloth upon it (the wound), and do not bind the nose with anything at all.
If the nasal bones are broken into pieces, or crushed, then it is necessary that you make an incision over them and extract those pieces using suitable instruments for the purpose. Then make a suture (stitch), and care for it with what coagulates (the tissue) and consolidates it using suitable ointments or poultices that are suitable for the purpose.
If it happens (that) there is a wound between the nose, then it is necessary to care for it with linen and administer a lead cannula until it heals.
Chapter on the restoration of the lower jaw when it breaks.
When the lower jaw breaks and there is no wound associated with it: examine whether its break is only external and not broken into two, and if it bends inwardly: then its diagnosis is easy.
Therefore, if the break is on the right side of the jaw, insert your left index finger into the sick person's mouth: similarly, if the break is on the left jaw, then insert your right index finger: and press with it the bulge of the fracture inwardly towards the outside with your other hand from the inner part of the bone evenly, and with it align it.
If the break of the jaw has already separated into two parts, then you must administer an extension from both parts according to their alignment: until it is possible for them to be aligned evenly.
If it happens that there is movement or dislocation of the teeth, then bind those that you hope will remain with a wire made of gold, silver, or silk. Then place soft wax on the broken jaw. Then place over it a double cloth, and place upon the cloth a large awl well made: or a piece of shoeleather equal in length to the jawbone.
Then bind it above as your preparation of the ligature requires and its application suits until it does not destroy itself: and order the sick one to take it quietly and with calmness: and place for him soft food for eating.
If you judge that it changes in some way by any means: then hurry and dissolve it on the third day. Then straighten out what is twisted from it, and plaster it with a plaster made of milled flour with egg white or with wheat bran after removing the soot: and place upon the plaster a soft woolen cloth.
As long as that plaster remains attached to it and the bone's position does not change, leave it undisturbed until it heals: or bind the fracture. For this fracture is frequently bound in three weeks.
If a hot abscess occurs meanwhile, then administer what we have said many times for the soothing of it until the abscess is removed.
And if the fracture is with a wound, be aware. If the fragment is separated from the bone or a tooth, be careful. Then use subtlety to remove those fragments of the gum that suit you for their removal using instruments.
If the opening of the wound is narrowed, then enlarge it with a leech according to your need.
Then when you remove the gum, and nothing remains from it, or if the opening of the wound is large: and if not, place upon it an ointment that suits for that purpose, and let it heal until it heals.
On the restoration of a broken Fork (Furcula).
Much indeed breaks the Fork from the anterior part near the shoulder, and its fracture is made according to one of three modes.
Either it breaks into two parts, beyond which occur protrusions (satagiae); and it is easier to restore this fracture.
The translation is: "It is thus, what happens in a fractured bone: and it is more difficult for restoration.
The translation is: "It is thus, what is a fracture with a wound.
The translation is: "And the operation in this case when there is no wound, it is to make present two assistants, so that one of them holds the supporting lever (Furcula) which follows the fracture, and the other stretches the neck towards the opposite side.
The translation is: "Then, make an equal fracture with your fingers until it has the required shape: and there should not be any projection nor depression.
The translation is: "If you need more extension, then it is necessary to place under the weak person's pillow a sphere made of cloth or wool, and let its size be according to your necessity: and extend, lift the lever, and press the sphere with your hand until an equal fracture, as required.
The translation is: "If you cannot pull the end of the lever outwards because it has reached deep inside: then, let the weak person lie on their neck and place a small plank under their shoulder in size, and press the assistant's arm downwards until the bone of the lever that is deep inside is lifted up to the surface: and then correct the fracture and make an equal one with your fingers.
The translation is: "If you feel a spasm from the lever and it moves: then, press on it and extract the spasm with ease.
The translation is: "If it is retained in the bone, then make an incision in that place with a tool prepared for this purpose after you have placed the instrument which holds the sifach under the lever. And the tool is made of wood or iron. The translation is: "And this is its shape.
The shell that lacks a hollow.
And let the width of it be according to what you need for it due to the size of the bone, and its smallness. Its length too, if possible for you, according to the operation.
And let it have two ends, as you see: and one end be wide, and the other narrow.
If, however, the fracture of the wound which you have cut when you extracted the splinter of bone is wide and you fear from a hot fistula: then bring together two lips of the wound with suture.
And if the fracture is small or you fear a hot fistula: then fill the wound with rags and pulverized charcoal according to the size of the wound incision.
If, however, a hot fistula occurs: then soak the rag in rose oil, vinegar, wine and place it on top of it.
But a bone setting without wound and without incision is as follows: place plaster made from flour milled with egg white on the bone. And place a soft woolen cloth over it. Then place a sphere under it if you need it. Then take the hemorrhoid, that is, a wooden roll which Saracens hold, which should be very long: and its width should be the size of one palm, or approximately that. Then place a double layer of charcoal on the woolen cloth and plaster. Then take a small awl made from fine wood, whose width is three fingers, and similarly long. Then thrust it through the cloth. Then thrust the cloth with the awl into the place of the hemorrhoid which falls on the fracture and wound. Then place the hemorrhoid on the fracture as it is rolled, and thrust it into the neck of the patient, both above the healthy part and below the injured part: and bring it all over so that the setting holds the fracture tightly: and this is what should not be hidden from you, and entirely, so that the awl does not move away from the fractured bone. It is necessary to observe the sick person every day. Whenever the bandage becomes soft and you see the splinter moving: then adjust it and tighten the bandage.
Then place the sick person's sleep on his back and put a small pill under his pillow during sleep, so that when he awakens, help will be summoned from his side. Therefore, break the collarbone by elevating the shoulder joint.
Or bind his arm to his neck and do not loosen the bandage unless it happens in the place where it is bound due to itching or abscess, for up to twelve days.
Then renew the plaster if you see his face turning towards it. And add to the constriction, and release it when it is healed and congealed.
The break in the collarbone is bound and supported as much as possible for 20 days; but sometimes it occurs in a lesser degree in some people.
Chapter on the repair of the spatula (thoracic plate).
The spatula rarely breaks, and in its wide place: and it is not broken from it except for its edges.
When the place from which it breaks and breaks in its middle is not known except by touch, then according to its shape prepare its treatment. And bring it back to its natural form by any means of skill that is possible for you.
Then place on it a little dust from the mill with egg white, and soft wool, and put above it a small piece of double-folded cloth. Then place on it a broad needle from thin wood according to the size of the spatula, or slightly larger but not too much.
But if there is a depression under the needle in any part of the spatula, then fill that depression with soft wool until the needle descends to the same level.
Then sew above it with long alum thread in a proper tension and the firmness of the needle until it is not moved from its place.
And examine the sutures every day. Whenever the suture becomes soft, sew it, and if the needle is moved from its place, let it lie weak on its healthy side.
The spatula is restored within twenty days, or within thirty days.
When this number is completed, then dissolve the suture: and you will be safe. For it is made from bones whose fracture is not feared, nor their solution.
But if a piece of bone protrudes and pierces the skin beneath it, then cut above it, and remove it. And do as we said in the treatment of the clavicle fracture by applying a hot poultice, if anything happens from it.
Chapter on the restoration of chest fracture.
Chest sometimes breaks in the middle of itself, and it happens rarely. Its extremities break or are crushed more often due to accidents.
When its middle breaks and it slopes downwards, accompanied by intense pain, difficulty in breathing, coughing, possibly vomiting blood, a hollow appears on the fractured bone that is not hidden to the senses, its restoration involves laying weakened on his back with a splint placed between his ribs.
Then press his shoulders and bring together the ribs on both sides or flanks with hands, and carefully carve it into alignment, as much as possible, until its shape returns to that of a bone, as required.
Then place an ointment and woolen cloth on top of it, and place a star-shaped bandage made from thin wood or ivory, or similar materials, wrapped in a fabric when you apply it. Then carefully bind it over the broken bone so that it does not dislodge; and walk around with the bandage following the roundness to the back several times, tightening it properly.
Then consider the binding at every hour and whenever it becomes soft, tighten it; and if the need arises for its dissolution due to itching, corrosion that occurs in the area, pain, or abscess, then hurry, unbind it, remove the ointment, and correct whatever has occurred with the treatment method.
Then reapply the ointment if you see it facing that way; and apply a tightening bandage until it heals.
Chapter on the restoration of ribs when they break.
Know that a rib fracture does not occur except in large areas following the back and from the anterior part of its extremities. It does not happen to them (the bones) abrasion, unless because they are cartilaginous. And the knowledge of it is not hidden from the sense during examination with fingers.
And its restoration is made so that it is set in a fracture with the fingers according to possible manner, until it corresponds to the shape as it should be. Then apply ointment, and bind the broken bone with an astragal if necessary.
If the fracture is of the ribs inclined toward the inside: then it happens a strong painful suffering to the weak, and puncture, as the puncture that occurs in him who has pleurisy: because the bone pierces the pleura, and also to him it happens difficulty breathing, and coughing, and vomiting blood many times: and this is difficult cure.
And the ancients have been skilled in this with many skills: and among them are those who say that you should put foods for the weak which generate inflammation and bloating, so that the stomach is inflamed and extended, and pushed the fracture towards the outside.
But we avoid that a cause arises for making a quick pustule, if it does not happen. If it has already happened, then you add (something) to it.
And some of them say: place a bladder on the location, then squeeze with force, and it is more similar to reasoning. However, it is feared that it attracts bladder superfluities to the location, due to the dispositions of weakness.
And some of them say: the location should be covered with wool that is already submerged in hot oil: and fleas are placed between the ribs until it is full: so that the binding is equal when you roll it according to roundness.
Then the sick person is to be treated for pleurisy with food and medicine.
If the disease causes severe constriction and the person cannot bear it, or if we pierce with a lancet and wound the membrane, and we fear for the sick person: then it is necessary that we make an incision over the spot, and expose the broken rib. Then we place on it an instrument which seals the sifac (whose description has been given earlier), and cut the bone with ease, then we cut it itself.
Then we bring together two edges of the wound if it is large with suture: and treat it with plasters until it heals.
If by chance an abscess occurs which is hot, then hurry and apply mustard seeds in rose oil on the spot: and treat the sick person with whatever suits the abscess.
Meanwhile, also rest on the side where he sleeps until it heals.
Section on the restoration of a fracture of the spine and neck.
Fracture sometimes occurs in the bones of the neck, and it rarely happens that they are rubbed [together]; and similarly with the vertebrae of the back as well. When it happens to someone that they are fractured and he wishes to know whether he will be healed or not: then consider [the situation]. For if you see that his hands are already softened, stunned and dead; and he cannot move them, extend them or close them; and when you press them with your fingers and pierce them with a needle, he does not feel it nor feels pain in them: then you will know that they will not heal, and in many cases, it is mortal.
If he moves them and feels compression with his fingers and the prick [of the needle], then you will know that the hump of the bone is sound, and that the weak one will be healed with treatment.
If it happens in the vertebrae of the back like the aforementioned [case], and you wish to know whether he will be healed or not: then consider his feet; then if you see that they are softened and the same thing occurs as in the hands; then when he lies on his back, wind comes out without his will; and when he lies on his stomach, wind comes out without his will; and when he tries to relieve himself, he cannot: then you will know that it is mortal. Therefore, do not labor in his treatment [of the fracture].
But if nothing of that kind happens to him, it is less severe.
And the treatment for his fracture that occurs in this way is to apply a plaster of warm emetics; thus placing oil of rose on the rubbed vertebra, or with pig's bladder soaked in vinegar.
Place it on that place several times a day until the warm emetic subsides; then place one of the strengthening and drying plasters, and bind it tightly over it with a bandage; and instruct the weak one to rest quietly and peacefully. He shall not sleep on the part where he found pain until it is cured.
If it happens during the fracture of a bone called Sathaiatum or something that has already separated from it: then one must remove it by incision on the skin and take it away. Then gather together two lips of the wound if it is large with suture. Then take care of it (the wound) with some ointment until it is cured.
If the last bone called Alhausos breaks and it is at the end of the tail: then one must insert the thumb of the left hand into the anus, and with the other hand one should adjust the broken bone as much as possible, according to the second alignment.
Then place it on (the broken bone) and take it away; and care for the wound with the remedy that has been mentioned before until it is cured.
Chapter on the restoration of anus.
Chapter XI.
Rarely do bones in the anus break. If they do break, the fracture of these bones is not anything but a comminution at their extremities; and they are split lengthwise, and they slope towards the interior; and there occurs pain and weakness in the place, and puncture; and the leg that breaks becomes numb.
And the restoration of it (the anus) is to extend your hand over it until you feel its shape at the breakage.
If the breakage is only at its extremities, then adjust that broken part according to the alignment prepared for you until it resembles its natural shape.
If the breakage is in length and already slopes towards the interior: then make the weak one lie on his stomach until the restoration of that broken part is prepared for you. When you have adjusted it (the broken bone), place an ointment on it; then place a splint made of wood or hide over it, and tie the bandage around it so that you do not fear the displacement of the fracture or the removal of the splint.
And equal the hollows of them with that which fills it until it is grasped by the ligature, according to equality; and command the sick one to sleep on his back or on his healthy side.
If, however, it happens that an abscess is hot: then cease from extending it and its healing until the hot abscess settles; and place on it what he sits upon, as before, then return to its healing; and tie it as necessary.
If, however, it happens that there is a fracture of bone, or it is broken, extract it.
[Section on the healing of fractures] Assistant.
The assistant is that which is between the elbow to the head of a spatula.
If it is broken, then its healing is according to one of two methods.
One is: that you take a flat piece of wood of medium thickness, in this shape, which follows.
And two ligaments shall be tied at both ends.
Then it is suspended from a raised place: and the sick person shall sit on the seat. Then let his broken arm be thrown upon a log until it reaches the handle of that log, where the wound of the log is attached.
Then suspend something heavy above it so that the assistant stretches it towards the lower parts. The physician then presses the fracture with his hands until it returns, as is required.
There is another way, namely, that the sick person lies on his back and his hand is suspended from his neck with a rope. Then order two assistants, one to hold the broken bone with his hands above it; and let the other hold the lower part of it, and each of them should stretch their respective parts.
If you wish for a stronger extension, then tie a rope under the fracture and above it with another; and let each of the two assistants stretch their respective parts to the two sides.
If therefore the fracture is near the end of the humerus, then a rope should be placed under the pulley and another towards the elbow.
And similarly, if the fracture is near the elbow: then a rope should be placed over that place and on the elbow itself.
Then press the fracture easily without effort, until it aligns as required and is applied well; then tie it if no warm abscess occurs.
If, however, a warm abscess occurs, then release the bandage for seven days. Place over it a shred of linen soaked in vinegar and rose oil until the abscess calms down; then tie it; and the method of tying is to apply a plaster over the fracture.
Then place a new cloth wrap over the plaster. Then bend the arm over the support and place your open hand on your shoulder.
Place a cloth and tie the arm to the support so that the arm remains in position of the stars; if it does not prevent you from anything or anything from the broken bone does not move towards you.
If you fear that something may change on you from a broken bone, then use trephines: and it is, that you place the trephine upon the fracture itself which is broader and stronger than the other trephines. Place a trephine of any width equal to the length of the finger for each trephine: and let the length of the trephines be according to the fracture, with an addition of three fingers from all sides.
Then tie a ligature over the trephines as I said at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is, that your ligature over the fracture site should be tight. And as the fracture extends more, the ligature should be less tight.
If you see the positions of the trephines and the ligature, as we said, at the hour when you restore the limb, then do so: and if you fear a warm abscess, then release the ligature and the trephines until the seventh day, as we said.
Then consider the ligature for three days so that itching, swelling, or food may not reach the limb due to the obstruction of the tightening of the ligature. Correct then everything as we said.
If you are confident that nothing will happen from it: then do not loosen the ligature, unless after three days or more.
And let the sick person lie on his back and place his hand on his stomach: and put under him a wool-filled cradle full of wool equally.
And consider at every hour of the night or day, so that the shape of the broken member is not loosened or the ligature softened. Correct then everything with your power.
And place the food of the sick person according to the order we gave, so that the food is subtle at first, until the bone begins to harden: then it is necessary that you increase his food.
In the custom of assistance and the leg, it is to be bound for forty days; then, therefore, it should be loosened, and a bath given, and it should be treated with ointments that correct it.
If the fracture is foul, to be scraped; then do not loosen the bandage and splints from it for fifty days, and until two months.
Chapter on the restoration of the arm fracture.
The arm is made up of two bones, which are called the two humeri.
One of them is small; and that is the one following the thumb. And the other is large, and that is the one placed below the small one from the lower part.
Therefore, perhaps only the larger humerus is fractured, or perhaps both are fractured.
When the smaller humerus is fractured above, then the restoration of it is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the larger humerus is fractured below, its fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And it is worse if both bones are fractured at the same time.
If the bone that is fractured is the smaller humerus above, then it is suitable for the physician at its restoration, that he places an extension smaller with ease, until it equals itself.
But if the larger humerus is the one that is fractured; then it is necessary that he places a stronger extension very much.
And it is necessary that he places the hand shape at its restoration, and its extension extended above the pulley. And let the thumb of the hand be higher than all the fingers; and let the inferior joint be lower than all the fingers or the others; and let the weak one sitting squarely above itself, and let the pulley be before it, or directly in front of it, in height, so that the weak one does not fall into labor.
Then, the servant extends the arm from its lower part, either with his hand, or with a rope. And another servant stretches it likewise above.
Then the physician sets the bone, so that he can restore it to its best possible shape.
If there are fragments in a broken bone, then make an effort to put each fragment back into its place by your own strength.
If it appears that a fragment is separated and pierces the skin, and there is no hope for restoration: then seal over it, and remove it according to the method we have mentioned earlier.
If there is a fracture with a wound, I have already written a separate chapter about this, so take care of it from there.
If a hot abscess occurs at the beginning of your treatment, then prepare a linen cloth with beeswax made from rose oil, and white wax, of a medium thickness between thick and thin: and bind the cloth over it tightly using a fine thread, until the abscess subsides: then remove the beeswax, and apply a prepared plaster made from milled powder with egg white. Then place the awls, and let the awl placed over the fracture be slightly wider and stronger.
And know that the number of awls for an arm is six in various arrangements: whether it be a fracture in one hole, or in two holes at the same time.
Then place your stronger and more powerful splint over the location of the fracture, and place a splint that is slightly longer on the upper part or lower part of the fracture, or place a softer splint for it slightly, according to what was mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And let the cloths that are wound around the fracture be soft and damp; they should not be hard or rough. And let the thread with which it [the wound] is to be tied be made from flax, of a medium thickness between fine and coarse, as we have said.
Consider the member (limb) and the ligament after days. If something occurs that requires correction, such as itching of the member (limb): then it is necessary to anoint the limb with warm embrocation until the itch subsides, and leave the limb unbound until it has subsided, and only then tighten again.
If the ligature has already been softened and the bone removed and similar things have happened: then you should correct it with your skill, and consider again.
If nutrition is prevented from reaching the member due to an excess of ligature, then it is necessary to soften it a little and leave it for some days until it reaches its nourishment, and then tighten it again.
But if nothing of the kind happens to the sick man which we have said, then it is not necessary to undo (loosen) it unless after twenty days or about that time.
Then the hand of the sick man is to be suspended to his neck. And let his neck be equal (straight), and keep it firmly fixed against violent movements. Make him sleep on his back.
Know that this fracture will heal in thirty or forty days, and perhaps it may heal within twenty-eight days. Everything depends on the dispositions of the temperaments (humors) and the dispositions of one's abilities.
Chapter on the restoration of fractures: Hand.
Know that a scissor's hand and the fingers are seldom fractured. It does not happen to him unless he is rubbed frequently.
When the foot is fractured or rubbed, then the sick man should sit on a square seat and a seat be provided for him in accordance with his height. Then place his hand upon it extended.
The assistant shall then extend the broken bones and the physician shall set them, until they join together with good union.
Then it is necessary that he places a plaster and a woolen dressing, if there does not occur a warm abscess. Then place a probe (astella) on top according to the size of the place, and you have already wrapped it in linen cloth.
If the fracture is around the lower part, about the heel of the foot, then make a splint similar to a ball from cloth; and command the patient to bend upon it with his broken foot; then bind it with a long cloth.
And let the probe (astella) be made of calfskin, with softness, to apply it to the skin of the sole; and bind it as necessary.
If the fracture is at the outer side, then it is necessary that you place a probe (astella) on top and another probe from below on the foot; and let the hand be open and straight. Then apply the splint as the hand is rolled; extend it between the fingers with a lacing.
If there occurs a fracture in any knuckle of the finger, if it is the thumb, then set it as necessary. Then bind it with the palm; if there are any, place a straight probe, smaller than the length of the fracture, so that it may be elevated and not moved.
If the fracture is in the remaining fingers, such as the middle one, index finger, ring finger or little finger, then set it and bind it with a healthy finger that follows it; or all of them are bound according to continuity; this is better. Or place a smaller probe on top as we said in the thumb.
And consider in your restoration, and after the disposition of warm abscesses; and oppose it with what is necessary, when something occurs from that, according to its narrative.
On the restoration of a hip fracture.
The thighbone (Femur) is often broken and exposed to the senses because it turns towards the inner and posterior parts.
And its restoration is done in such a way that a ligament is tightened above the fracture, and another ligament below the fracture. The patient is then laid supine on his face.
Then, all the limbs are to be extended towards their respective parts according to equality; and this, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
If, however, the fracture is near the root of the hip, then let there be a ligament towards the neck part so that the extension falls upwards; and another ligament below the fracture.
And similarly if the fracture is near the knee, then let there be a ligament near the knee so that the extension falls downwards.
Then the physician equalizes the bone with both hands until he reduces it to its original shape; and the bone is joined by a good union.
Then it is necessary that you apply a plaster and a bandage if there is no abscess in the limb. If an abscess occurs in it: then let it be left for some days until the abscess subsides, and then return to your treatment.
The bandage should be as follows. It is necessary to wind a hard material, that is a bandage, over the fracture; and double it twice or thrice; and there remains an excess of it. Then wind the leg until the heel reaches the root of the foot.
And insert a long thread between the hip and the leg near the knee towards the lower parts; and the ends of the thread come from the upper parts of the two limbs.\] Then wind over the leg and hip the excess from the bandage.
Then place a star (awl) over the thigh at the site of the fracture and place one star (awl) over the leg's bone.
Then fill in the gaps between thigh and shin with soft cloth, so that it fits tightly (to hold the fractured bones together).
Then begin with a firm ligature from the middle over the site of the fracture with three or four wraps. And as you extend the ligature away from the site of the fracture, let your ligature be less tight and softer, and looser.
Then direct the threads at both ends, which you inserted between thigh and shin. Tie together with each end three auls (awls) that are above (the area). Then go with both ends of the thread to the lower parts until you reach the ankle of the foot. And tie together also the ends of the awls with each end, so that the ligature is not dislocated from its place.
Then release the ligature when necessary, so that it does not cause rot (gangrene), boil (abscess), swelling or similar to the member. If something happens with it. Therefore approach with the solution of it (the affected area) and correct whatever has happened there entirely, as we have said many times.
If the splinter is in the bone that pierces it (the flesh): then it is necessary to equalize it (remove the splinter), if it is possible for you. And if not, plug over it and remove it; take care of the one who preceded it (the surgeon) in memory until it heals.
And sometimes this thigh fracture is ligated beyond what the auls (awls) are added, as we said in help for the arm and leg.
However, in repairing this fracture, it does not happen that a lame limp occurs. If it (the limb) is only restored, provided that another leg is not applied to it, then it is necessary that the one to whom it belongs will always limp.
Know that the hip is bound for fifty days, or a little more, or a little less; all this depends on the diversity of temperaments and other conditions.
Chapter on the restoration of the fracture of the knee joint.
Know that the knee joint rarely suffers a fracture but often suffers wear and tear.
But if it does suffer a fracture, then it is not other than a split or a hole in one of its parts. And it is with an ulcer and without an ulcer. It stands on the whole thing with feeling.
The restoration of it is thus that the broken parts are brought together with the fingers until they meet and are joined according to what is possible by means of matching and ease, and construction.
Then place a plaster made of oatmeal flour or milled grain with egg white on it; and place a round stopper over it if needed; then bind it above with a suitable bandage.
Then consider all the conditions that we have mentioned in other fractures; such as a hot abscess and similar things; so that you counter any occurrence with what is appropriate to it until it heals.
Chapter on the restoration of a leg fracture.
Know that there are two bones in the leg, one of which is large and called the shinbone (Crus); and the other is slender and called the anklebone (Focile).
And it happens that in the case of the leg bones, as with the bones of the arm, fractures occur; and for this reason their restoration is made equally, and the operation is one and the same.
If two bones are broken together, the leg is turned to all parts.
If a bone is broken more subtly, the leg is turned towards the anterior part.
If a bone is broken coarsely, you will find it below; and it is from those that do not deceive you.
Then administer extension, equality, and ligature of the stars according to that equally.
However, if there is a foul fracture of the leg with many seithagi, it should have less extension and be softened more by your application.
And in the leg there is something more than in the arm in operation; and it is that when you tie the stars, and complete your entire work, then take two bands from pine wood, which are used in the making of solariums placed under tables; and they are made from the spatulas of the palm; and choose from them those with small thickness and not too small; and let their length be according to the length of the leg from the knee to below.
Then fold over each cloth twice, according to its length. And place one part of the leg on one side and another part on the other; and they should be from the knee to below the foot.
Then bind two bands in three places, from the two extremities and middle. For when binding is forbidden, the leg inclines to the right and left, and remains in a good stance.
And indeed a cradle made of wood should be applied according to the length of the leg, and the leg should be placed into it so that it is restrained from movement.
And certainly it must be done in a fracture when there is an ulcer in it properly.
Then consider the leg every day, and strive with it to the utmost from apostemate, or inflation, or any others. If therefore something happens from those [things], then oppose it with what is necessary, until it heals, if God wills it.
On the Fracture of Bones in the Foot and Fingers.
Indeed, it does not happen that the calcaneum is broken entirely.
The bones of the foot indeed sometimes suffer a break, and it happens less often with the fingers; they do not break except by repeated impact in several positions.
If a fracture occurs in the bones of the foot and you see that the bone rises above itself, then lay the injured foot on the ground prepared like one walking.
Then stand you there, and place your foot upon that which is raised by those bones; afterwards press, or trample it; and hold it even, until it returns to its places.
Then place ointment and wax upon it; and place a small table underneath the foot with two flat heads.
Then bind it with a bandage suitable for the foot when you have wrapped it with linen and other things needed.
When three or four days have passed since then, loosen the bandage. For you will find the bones evened out if they are broken or displaced. Therefore the operation is as you see it.
If fingers are broken, then restore them and even them as I have told you in the restoration of the fingers of the hand. Then place a star-shaped splint on your finger according to the length of the finger, and it should be wider by a little; then place under the foot the table I have told you about, and bind it with a suitable bandage.
If what breaks is two or more bones, then place a star-shaped splint over the entire finger wrapped in linen; bind the table under the foot; and let the table stand on one side of the foot outside the waist to hold it firmly with good support.
It is appropriate for you to remember that you should consider all the things I told you about fractures from the accidents mentioned previously; and to apply each of these accidents with what is suitable.
On the fracture of a woman's jaw, and the bone of the breastplate, and the man's phallus.
When a woman's jaw is fractured, then make her sit upright. Then lean her towards the back of that side a little, and hold it in place.
Then fill her jaw with linen cloth until it is full; and make a ball of it within the jaw.
After that, strike the woman on the back, and rub the fracture gradually. For this reason, the linen cloth comes out until it forms a ball before the jaw opening, and the broken bone returns to its place.
Then place some small stones on her back. Whenever she wishes to drink, let her remove the linen cloth easily, then drink; and reduce it according to how you first inserted it; then return to the small stones first.
Do this for seven days or approximately, because the bone will heal itself.
And if you wish, take the bladder of an ox and tie a reed tube around its opening, and insert the entire bladder into her jaw. Then blow through the tube with force until the bladder inflates within the jaw, because the fracture will return to its place.
Then it is filled with linen cloth and remains for some days as we have said, until it heals.
When the breastplate bone of a man or woman is fractured, then administer the same treatment as we have said for the bone of Ancha.
And it is not concealed straight [bone] in such fractures which scarcely touch [it], especially when there is something of study or use involved.
Understand my book well, for the fracture itself signifies to you in many dispositions the way of the straight [bone] in its restoration and its constriction; therefore understand it.
When the penis of a man is broken, take the neck of a goose and insert the rod into it. Then place on top of it a covering made from cloth, and wrap it up, and let it be for three days or about that, and it will heal if God wills it.
Chapter on the restoration of bone fractures when there is a wound.
It is suitable for him who has a fracture accompanied by a wound, especially if it is large like the hip bone or humerus, to hasten and call the blood-letter immediately; if the conditions of phlebotomy are testified to you as we have previously mentioned.
If the wound exudes blood, then it is necessary for you to hasten to its excision, so that you powder over it a mixture of chalk and lime; if no other [substance] is present to you.
Then begin the restoration of the fracture on that same day; and do not delay it, unless an abscess occurs.
If a hot abscess occurs, then wait for the restoration of it until the ninth day, until the abscess settles down. And do not approach him on the third and fourth days at all, for you would cause him harm.
If the broken bone is protruding above the skin exposed, then it is necessary to compress it and bring it back to its place with your hands easily and with little extension.
If however the extension is not prepared, and the same is equal to your hands: then reduce it with this instrument. And is an instrument that is made of iron, the length of which is the amount of seven or eight fingers, and its width according to the size of the wound. And therefore it is necessary for the physician to receive three or four according to the quantity that he needs for this in all types of fractures.
And let it be round; and let there be a small thickness inside it so that it does not double up against the instrument during the operation within an hour; and let it have sharp ends with an edge on its ends. And the top of it should be thick from the middle to the bottom.
This is its shape.
In this operation, the sharp end should be placed on the protruding bone's edge; and push it with one blow until the bone returns and becomes equal to some degree.
Try then to align the fracture's ends facing each other.
If the ends of the fracture are subtle and do not accept the instrument well, then it is necessary to cut the bone's edge until the instrument can grasp it.
But if you cannot reduce them with the instrument as we have said completely, then cut it with the saw that suits it from the incision tools that we have mentioned; or cut it with a pair of scissors in any way possible for you.
Then file away what remains on the bone from roughness and small layers.
When the bone is reduced, and after the reduction of it finds a weak point causing intense pain, then you know that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
Make it. Thou conferrest great aid to the sick one, when thou complete the restoration that thou dost make of the bone.
Then immerse the cloth in stiptic wine, black. And indeed it is appropriate in summer. And do not place over the wound black things, nor anything that contains oil: so that it does not cause putrefaction and corruption at the wound.
Then administer the astella in the hour of your completion of the bone restoration. And leave the detected wound: so that you cut with the forceps a hole in the swellings according to the size of the wound.
Take care to avoid at all costs that you bind the wound during the fracture. For many times have foolish doctors done this: and they caused death or corruption, or gangrene, that is, pus, upon the sick. Let your binding be soft, gentle, diverse binding from other fractures.
If the wound is bad or the wound is large and you fear certain evil accidents that we have mentioned, and you find pain suspended at the place: then it is not necessary that you place astella over it. And make swellings from stiff cloth in place of astella and bind it with them.
But when it is one day or two days and you see that the wound begins to produce pus: then take away from above the cloth that you placed over it with wine. Then administer linen and ointments with which it is our custom to treat wounds: such as tetrapharmacon ointment and similar ones.
It is appropriate for you that you loosen the binding. Consider the wound every day at morning and evening until it is consolidated and healed.
It is necessary that the limb should be prepared in such a way that pus runs out of it easily towards the lower parts.
If several days pass without the wound healing and the pus is not expelled from it, then you will know that there are small bones there. Therefore, one should examine the wound with a probe. As many of these small bones as are separated, remove them and extract them. And those which are not separated and pierce the limb and cause pain, try to cut them and remove them in any way possible for you.
If there occurs a tumor or ulcer on the wound, that is to say, if it is an abscess or another form of corruption and putrefaction: then it is necessary to apply to each of these with what is suitable for its cure, which was described in its chapter.
And in the discussion from which my speech is required when a bone breaks and protrudes on the limb, such as the hip or the arm, and similar large bones, it is not to be presumed that one can attract it, for often death occurs from this; rather leave it alone until it has rotted away.
Perhaps it will fall off by itself after twenty or thirty days.
Therefore, treat the wound if you see fit; otherwise, leave it alone.
Chapter on the treatment of swelling in a fracture.
Often this swelling occurs in the healing of a fracture. Most often it is near the joints; and it corrupts the shape of the limb. And perhaps it prevents the limb from performing its natural functions.
Therefore, if the swelling is new, administer medications that have astringent properties, such as aloes, olibanum, myrrh, sarcocolla, acacia and similar remedies.
Thus, take some or all of them, and suture them with sticky wine, or with egg white, or with vinegar; and place them on the knot, and tighten them well on the good constriction itself.
And leave the suture, do not loosen it for many days. Then loosen it and make another one until the knot is removed.
Or suture it well on the lead sheet. For lead has the property of removing everything that is feared from the limbs.
If the knot has already turned to stone and been hardened, and urgently calls for its removal: then make an incision above it on the superior part, and cut off the excess protruding; or grind it with certain rasps until it is removed; and care for it until it heals.
\paragraph{On the treatment of when the limb is restored, and remains subtle after that, not according to its own nature.}
When a bone fracture is restored and the limb remains subtle and weak after that, it should not be made unless for many reasons. One of the reasons is the excessive solution and tying of the ligament not as required; or due to the excess tightness of the ligaments until it prevents nourishment from reaching the limb; or due to the excessive embrocation at an inappropriate time; or due to the scarcity of blood in the body of the sick and its weakness.
And the treatment of this is, to feed the sick, and make his body soft until the blood multiplies in him. And administer a bath. And introduce joy and happiness over him.
Then place pitch on the limb: so that the pitch attracts much nourishment to it. Apply embrocation to it with warm water until the nourishment runs, and return it to its natural shape.
On the cure of fractured bones when they are restored twistedly.
When a restored member, having been cured of its deformity and the protrusion of a fractured bone or nodule, becomes disfigured due to such condition; yet, it does not prohibit the natural operation of the limb; then, it is not necessary that you heed the speech of one who proposes breaking the limb anew.
And indeed there have been many foolish physicians and restorers, who did this in our land.
And this operation is highly condemnable, leading to death or great fear, indeed it should be feared as lethal.
However, if the limping and nodulation are recent; then it is necessary to immerse [the affected area] in warm water, into which have been boiled softening herbs such as elder leaves, its root, sweet clover, and similar plants. And make for it a plaster with a softening plaster like well-made dyachilon.
Or take the mucilage from elder root, pound it with chicken fat or sesame oil; and apply it as a plaster.
Or take the ripe figs, pulverize them with dove droppings and similar remedies that reduce hardening.
And sometimes the nodule dissolves through persistent, old-fashioned, and gentle rubbing, which is done with moist hands. And exercise the limb fully in all directions daily.
If the deformity has already occurred, and it has become strong, and has turned into a stone, and there is necessity for treatment with iron; then it is necessary to cut off its upper part, and to dissolve the continuation of the bone, and to incise what protrudes from the nodule or bone with subtle chisels; and apply ease in this with diligence and care.
On Speech in Dislocations.
Dislocation is the displacement of some joints from their place. Why it should be prevented from moving and becomes disfigured in shape (or appearance) of the member. And causes falling upon the weak pain, and severe injuries.
When therefore dislocation occurs to someone, then it is necessary to hasten for its restoration: nor should one delay it at all.
For if delayed, the location will be lost, and perhaps with it the reduction of dislocation.
Therefore, it should not be moved or extended during its relocation hour (or time of resetting). For because it often causes falling into a spasm, and painful injuries.
But when this happens, then it is necessary that you hasten to the bloodletting of the weak. Then it should be left until the abscess subsides (or becomes calm).
Then let the limb be wrapped with warm water and oil. Then it should be reduced gently.
And all the limbs should be treated together, with that one whose recollection comes back to its place (or position).
I myself have already ordered the chapters on dislocations, even according to what precedes in fracture, from the upper part of the body down to the lower part.
Chapter on care for lower jaw dislocation.
The two lower jaws are seldom dislocated.
And dislocation of both occurs in one of two ways. Or they are removed from their places slightly and softened slightly (or temporarily).\] Et aut dislocatur una ex eis, altera autem restat in situ suo. The dislocation is to be reduced by integration, completely, until it softens towards the chest, until the saliva of the weak runs and it cannot hold her, nor can it close its own mouth, and his tongue is rolled up with speech.
When the dislocation is slight, then, by itself, it returns in many ways with a little effort.
If the dislocation is complete and whole, then it must be quickly administered the reduction of it and should not delay greatly; and there is to be one who holds the head of the weak, and the physician inserts his thumb of one hand into the root of the dislocated jawbone if the dislocation is from one side. Or, he inserts two thumbs at once, if the dislocation is from both sides. And the remaining fingers of his hands are to be outside, with which it is equally held.
And order the weak one to cleanse his jawbones and purify them for eating in every respect. And the physician presses and pushes the jawbone until it returns to its place.
If the reduction is difficult, especially if both jawbones are dislocated at once: then administer vaporization with hot water and oil until the reduction of both becomes easy; do not delay the reduction of both in any way, as we have said.
When they return and align, and the mouth of the weak one is closed, and they do not become soft: then place over them small pieces of cloth, with beeswax that has been made, and scented oil.
Then it is to be bound gently with soft ligaments.
And the sleep of the weak one is on his back; and his head is standing between two pillows so that it does not move to the right and left; nor should he be forced or compelled to chew something; rather, let his food be soft sorbitio, until when the pains are removed, and the jawbone is loosened: then he can eat what he wants, and it is administered gently.
Do not let him eat or drink, nor speak, at his mouth operation, until the mandible is healed and it is cured.
If the reduction of the two mandibles becomes difficult when they are dislocated at some hour and do not return to their places: then frequently fevers occur, and an abscess continuous; and perhaps the weak stomach dissolves, and perhaps it vomits pure cholema.
When you see that (thing), then know that it is a disaster, and he who has it frequently dies within ten days.
Section on the reduction of dislocation of clavicle, and the extremity of the humerus.
The clavicle is not dislocated from the inside side, because of its connection with the chest. And it is sometimes dislocated towards the outside: and this becomes clear to sensation.
The restoration is that the weak one lies on his back and extends his arms. Then empty the place with your firm grasp, for it will return to its place.
Then place the plaster on it and the little pills, and press it down.
The extremities that follow the elbow do not dislocate except rarely. If it is dislocated on any day: then it must be returned to its place and made equal according to what was said, and what has been prepared for you.
Then place the plaster on it, and pills, and a bandage. And command the weak one, to hold onto tranquility and rest until he is healed.
With this same treatment, the elbow joint is returned when it also separates from its place.
Chapter on the reduction of shoulder dislocation.
Know that the shoulder does not dislocate except in three ways.
One is, it dislocates to the side of touching towards the lower part. The second is, it dislocates towards the chest. And perhaps it is dislocated to the upper part of the shoulder: and that happens rarely.
It does not dislocate towards the back, due to the spine of the scapula. It does not dislocate towards the front, due to nerves.
Most often it dislocates and exits towards the lower side, turned towards the side of touching; especially in those whose flesh is scanty. Because it quickly comes out in them and enters quickly.
In those whose flesh is numerous, there is contrarily its counterpart, namely because it exits with difficulty, and enters with difficulty.
And perhaps it happens to some people a blow or accident and the humerus is amputated with hot amputation tool and it is believed because of that it has already been dislocated.
Therefore, one should test it so as to be certain of its truth and then proceed with its treatment.
A dislocation is recognized when it faces downwards towards the tip of the elbow; and you should distinguish between a dislocated shoulder and a healthy one. For you find among them an obvious difference, and in the head of the shoulder you find a hollow, and beneath the tip of the elbow at touch, it seems like an egg; and an invalid cannot bring his hand to his ear, nor move it with all movements.
And similarly, if it is dislocated towards the chest or upwards: then you find a clear touch and it is not hidden.
This kind of dislocation can be easily reduced when it is recent or if the child is weak.
The reduction of this kind is that the hand should lift itself towards the upper part. Then place your thumbs under the tip of his, and lift the joint with strength to its place; and let the hand lift itself and stretch it upwards. Then put it down below, for it will return quickly by itself.
But if it does not return according to what has been said, and there are many days since the dislocation occurred: then it is necessary to bathe the invalid in warm water, and administer an embrocation that softens and soothes, such as if they boil the roots of althea, fenugreek and mellilotus in water, and administer it.
Then, the sick one shall lie on his back: and let there be placed under his navel sphere and wool, which is equal in thickness between softness and tightness.
Then the physician shall place his heel upon the sphere, and lift up the head of the arm with force, and draw the hand of the sick one downwards.
And if you wish, bring him back in this manner: and it is, that you make a man who is taller than the sick one to stand by his side, and insert his arm under the navel of the sick one: and lift up the navel of the sick one towards the upper part, until the sick one is suspended in the air. And another servant shall draw the hand of the sick one to the lower part of his belly. If the sick one is light: then it is necessary that something else be suspended with him to weigh him down; for a dislocation returns immediately.
And another method is also returned: and it is, that a long piece of wood be formed on the ground, whose head is round in shape, like the handcuff of a miller; but not too thick or too thin. Then let there be placed under the sick one, after the head of the wood is placed, soft cloths: and the sick one shall stand according to the length of the wood.
Then his hand shall be stretched out to the lower part from one side, and his body shall be extended from the other side with force: for the union returns to its place with speed.
If the reduction is difficult through all that has been said, then use this remedy: and it is, that you take a piece of wood whose length is the size of two arms, and its width is the size of four fingers, and its thickness is the size of two fingers; and let its head be round to facilitate the entrance into the deep navel, according to this shape.
Then, soft cloths shall be bound over the rounded head, so as not to injure the weak wood.
Then, place under the trembling of the weak one, so that the hand or arm can be extended over the wood until the lower parts; and tie the wood with assistance, and the arm and the end of the hand.
Then, place the arm over the step of the ladder at its width, and extend the hand to the lower parts, and let the remaining body be suspended from the other side. For this reason, join immediately.
When the opening for it is completed in whatever way possible: then it is necessary that you place under the trembling sphere of equal quantity from wool. Then place the prepared plaster from milled powder with olibanum and egg white over the whole shoulder as it is turned upside down.
Then bind under the trembling tie of suitable size; and roll up the bandage over the plaster above; and attach his hand to his neck, and let it not be moved for seven days.
It is necessary that a small amount of food be placed, until the limb is strengthened; since it is quicker for its healing.
Then, place it larger after seven days or five, and let it be shaken with motion. If it is strengthened and does not become soft, then it has been healed.
But if the joint is dislocated many times, because of the moisture that happens to it, or for another reason: then it is necessary, to administer cautery on it with three holes, according to what was mentioned in the chapter on cautery.
If all this is done and the bandage is loosened after seven days, and the joint does not become strengthened, and the plaster and tie are not placed over it again, and it does not become firm, and falls, and cannot be raised to above: then you will know that the nerves of the neck at the shoulder have been cut or stretched or softened; and you will know that the joint never heals in its place.
Dislocations that occur towards the chest and breast, or are reduced to the rear, are accomplished by pushing and extending with the hands, until it returns, and treatment and restraint are administered in it until it heals.
If pain or sluggishness in the limb occurs after healing, then the weak should use baths often, until the pain is relieved and it returns to its natural state.
Chapter on the treatment of shoulder dislocations.
Know that the union of the shoulder joint is dislocated with difficulty and also returns with difficulty; and indeed it is dislocated in all directions, but especially towards the front or rear.
And the dislocation of this joint will not be hidden from you; since it falls under your sight and touch, according to whatever form it is dislocated. And when you join a dislocated shoulder with a healthy one, the dislocation is revealed clearly to you. For the joint will be hollowed out, and it cannot double the arm, nor touch its own humerus with it.
And it is necessary that you hasten and reduce the dislocation immediately, before a warm abscess occurs.
For if a warm abscess occurs, the reduction becomes difficult; and perhaps it will not heal at all, especially when towards the rear. For of all kinds of dislocations, it is worse in every way, and more painful; and death often accompanies it many times.
And the restoration of this, when it can be reduced, is as follows: extend the hand with two hands; let the arm be spread out, and the hands of the doctor to be above and below the shoulder, and he himself should push the joint with his thumb or the root of his palm until it returns to its place.
If it is a dislocation to the front: then return it so that he separates his hand with his hand until he strikes with the root of his palm the elbow which is dislocated.
If the dislocation does not obey to reduction, then administer a violent and strong traction; and it is such that two attendants stretch the arm; and the two attendants also hold it, so that it is not moved during the traction.
Then the arm is turned to every part when it falls upon his hand, a cloth doubled long or a wide bandage is applied over it.
And when the doctor touches with his hand the joint, he anoints it with oil; so that it is from those which aid in lubricity of the joint with facility. Then push the joint forcefully until it returns.
After its reduction, it is necessary that a plaster be placed on it, containing the stiptic and the application of white wax; and to bind with a suitable ligature. Suspend the arm from the neck of the patient; and leave for some days.
Then loosen it. If the joint is fixed in its place, then loosen the bandage from it, and leave it.
And if you see the joint not bound well, then apply a new plaster, and bandage, and leave it for some days until it is bound. Then loosen it.
If, after the reduction of the joint, there is pain and its position is in motion: then administer humectation in bathing, and subtle friction, and compression, until it is lined, or place over the joint a goat's fat tail, that is, a thick grease. Then bind it and leave it on it day and night. Then remove it, and immerse him in the bath. When therefore it sweats, then rub the junction with moderate rubbing. Then again apply it to the same three times, with the beginning of the bath, until it becomes soft.
And if you wish, place upon it dung of sheep, moist and warmed with butter, and press upon it.
Make that frequently. It becomes soft, and returns to its former state.
Chapter on the cure of dislocation by Al-Zahrawi (Albucasis).
Mahassem's hand is often dislocated, that is, the junction between arm and hand.
The reduction of this dislocation is easy. Contrariwise, other joints. However, it is necessary that swiftness be in the reduction of this dislocation at the hour it occurs, before the place is evacuated; or a hot abscess may occur there.
And the reduction of this dislocation is that you place Mahassem's weak hand on a table and extend the hand of the assistant; and the physician shall place his palm over the prominence of the junction and push it until it returns.
However, it should be considered if the palm has already receded to the abdomen of the hand; then let the weak hand lie on a table during extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is proceeding towards the back of the hand, then its position should be inside the table, that is, the inner part of the hand should be placed on the table, so that the physician's hand falls upon the prominence of the junction.
If it returns immediately, that is good; and if not, then press it with an ointment sitting on a hot abscess, and leave it alone, do not repeat it.
For if it does not tolerate and you cannot perform the reduction of it after the days have passed, nevertheless the joint remains in its crooked state; and it does not harm the weak anything, except that the hand is softened and nothing can be pressed; then you will know that the nerves have been cut or rubbed.
The skill in it is only to tighten with a cautery near its failure. And perhaps that brings nothing additional.
Since Almahassem did not return, then place a plaster on top of him, as we have said. Then it is tightened and released for five days; afterwards it is loosened and released, and exercise the hands.
If its movement is hindered, and something heavy happens to it; then soften it with warm water, and rub it occasionally until it becomes soft.
Chapter on the treatment of finger dislocations.
When a finger is dislocated from any part, or a finger is dislocated to the back of the palm or its ventrum; then extend the finger and push the dislocation with your thumbs until it returns.
Then hold the head of the finger, and suspend it turned towards the side to which it is dislocated, and release it for two days.
Then suspend it and extend it until it becomes straight on that day itself.
When it is night, bind it according to the aforementioned description; and do not cease to release it during the day; and exercise it with movement; and bind it at night.
Do that for some days until it tightens.
And similarly do the same if the dislocation is towards the palm of the hand; and bind it to that part itself, and perform your first operation on it until it heals.
And similarly do the same when it is dislocated towards other parts.
Chapter on the treatment of spondylous dislocations of the back.
When anyone has a spondylous dislocation of the back or a complete dislocation of the neck, or many spondyles are displaced from their places; then there is no cure for it, because death approaches the sick one.
The sign of it is that the sickness of the person departs without will, and he cannot hold it; and many times some members of his are softened from it, or two legs of his, or two arms of himself, or one of them; or a vertebra is removed from its place.
Many times a removal occurs, which often happens in four parts.
Therefore, what is removed towards the backside is called Gibbositas.
The cure for it is to consider whether the Gibbositas occurs during childhood; then there is no treatment in it, nor healing at all from it.
However, the one that occurs by accident, or blow, or similar things, the ancient ones have already described the methods of treatment in a long discourse. It is unlikely to return when it advances.
I have already departed from that which excuses itself little by its proximity to my intention; and in my exposition of it, I formed the tool for it differently than they showed it and explained it.
Therefore, I say that there is no remedy, nor healing from the Gibbositas that occurs from the front side.
And similarly in the one that occurs on both sides.
And it is not treated from them unless the one that occurs on the back properly, as I say; and it is to be done that the sick person extends his face on a flat surface, almost against the wall; and a wet blanket is spread under him so as not to hurt his chest.
Then place a straight piece of wood fixed in the fireplace on the floor, at the end of the foot; and another piece of wood about his feet at the other end of the foot; and hold the man keep the wood, and it should not be fastened in the fireplace. One assistant holds the other.
Then, stretch over the head of the sick one, and bind a ligament gently but firmly beneath his chin; extend the end of the ligament to the wooden post that is beside his head; and tie it there.
Then stretch another ligament over his ankles, and above his knees, and at the shackles of his legs. Then gather all the ligaments, and tie them to another wooden post that is by his feet.
Then each assistant extends his ligament to the part of the wooden post, and let the two posts not be moved from their places where they are fixed; but they should not be made fast as we said.
And the physician places his hands on the patella (Spondyle) firmly until it returns; or he can place a board over it. Then connect his feet to the board until it returns.
If it does not return through this treatment, then take a board whose length is approximately three arms (about 4.8 feet or 1.5 meters). And drive a hole in the wall as mentioned near the sick one, into which the end of the board enters. Then place the middle part of the board over the patella (Spondyle). And let the physician put his feet on the other end. And bind it well until the patella (Spondyle) is dislocated and returns to its place.
Then, if you want, operate with a drill that is held by your hand; and it will be like driving two wooden posts into the ground at the head of the sick one on the last foot, whose length is an arm (about 1 cubit or 48 cm); and at his head on the last foot, two wooden posts.
And let the length between every post be a palm (about 9 cm); and it is now made that there is a hole where the drill runs through; and let every post be fixed firmly in the ground so that it does not move at all.
And a round stick is introduced: and it is a probe in which the ligament revolves in the two holes of the two sticks.
And at its end there is a hole, in which the stick is fixed, whose length is a palm with that which revolves; and similarly on the other two sticks.
Then tie the ligaments which you have tied in the chest of the sick man to the probe that is at his head; and the ligaments which you have tied in his legs to the probe that is at his feet.
Then stand by each probe the servant who will twist his hand with the linen cloth, with which the probe revolves; and the doctor ties the Spondile, as we said before.
This is the form of the probe that follows.
Then when the Spondile returns and the place is tied, then it is necessary that you put on an ointment of lime with egg white. Then apply a plaster (or lint). Then put on top of the ointment a star-shaped patch from wood, whose width is three fingers or around that. And its length is the amount that covers the humpback (or protrusion), and above the healthy part of the Spondile.
Then it should be tied with the ligament that we said before. And the sick man shall take a subtle nourishment until he is healed.
If something remains as a protrusion in the place at the end of healing: then it is necessary to administer softening and soothing medicines along with the use of the tablet that we said before for a long time. And now lead is applied on it (the Spondile).
Sometimes a protrusion occurs at the last Spondyle of the back; and it is thought to be a dislocation, and that bone which is now projecting. It is not appropriate to presume [treatment] with this medicine, for perhaps it will cause death.
Treatise on the Treatment of a Dislocated Shoulder.
Know that the joints of the ankle and shoulder do not occur except by dislocation; nor does what happens to the other joints happen to them through slight displacement and concavity.
Indeed, the joint of the ankle is dislocated in four parts. It is because it is dislocated to the front and back.
It is dislocated indeed more towards the inside; it is dislocated less to the front and back.
The treatment of dislocation towards the inside is: because when you join the weak foot to a healthy one, it is longer; and the knee of the weak one protrudes more than that of the healthy one, and the weak one cannot double his own foot at the groin; and there is a place following the groin which is pressed with something hot, therefore because the head of the hip has already reached there.
The sign of dislocation towards the outside is: that its symptoms are opposite to these symptoms.
The sign of dislocation towards the front is: that it stretches the healthy leg too much; however, it does not double without pain in the knee; and if he tries to walk on it, he cannot do so over it at the front; and he retains his urine, and his groin is pressed, and during walking his heel is stepped on.
The sign of dislocation towards the back is: that it does not stretch the knee, nor can it double before the groin doubles; and his leg is even shorter than the other one; and his groin is softened, and the head of the hip is at its place.
The reduction of this dislocation however is: that you should consider then if the dislocation is old, which has already become chronic with the one having it. Then if you administer the reduction of it and it remains according to its position; then there is no cure at all in that case. Therefore, it is not appropriate to presume him completely.
In the case of a recent dislocation, which belongs to one of the four types of dislocations: then proceed by pressing and stretching [the limb], moving it inward and outward, and shaking it left and right: perhaps it will return, and no further care is needed.
If it does not return by this method, then prepare a strong assistant, and let him stretch the leg from below: either with his hands, or with a strap that is tied above the knee: and another assistant should extend the hand above, so that he introduces his hands under the joints. Then, bind gently with a strap over the root of the pelvis, and hold the end of the other strap.
And the extension should be either from the front part of the groin to the neck region, or from the back to the back region.
The extension of all [limbs] should be uniform until the weak one is raised from the ground with his body and remains hanging.
And this type of extension is a common type among the four types.
If the dislocation returns with what I have said, it is good: and if not, then it is necessary for all types to apply what I will say about its treatment.
In the case of its own reduction when the dislocation is toward the inside, it is necessary for the weak person to lie on his healthy side. Then, place a strap over the root of the pelvis, where it is between the head of the pelvis and the area under the groin. Then extend the strap upward from the groin to a higher part of the body, to the neck region.
Then receive another strong assistant with his arms, and reduce the swollen area of the weak pelvis, and stretch it outward by means of a strong extension. Now, it returns to its own place.
This is an easier kind of treatment among the rest, by which this limb is reduced.
If it is excised over you and does not obey you for this kind of treatment at all: then it is necessary that two weak feet are bound together with a strong ligament, gently, on the two heels, and on the two knees; and let the length of each be four digits' width apart from its root; and let the injured leg be extended by more than twice the width of two digits.
Then the injured one is suspended above the head of a pole, which is in the house; and let its length from the ground be two forearms' width.
Then command a strong youth to strap the head of the hip; and suspend it with the injured one, another youth will do so; and another youth will push, strapping the hip with force, for the joining returns to its own place quickly.
In its own reduction, when it is a dislocation towards the outside, it is necessary that the injured one lies on a stool, according to what we have said about having a hump. And bind a bandage around its leg, the injured one's own, and its chest.
Then two poles are placed, one at his feet and another at his head. Then a pole is placed in the middle of the stool, firmly fixed; on which the injured ones are wrapped with wet cloths so as not to injure it; and let there be a pole between its hips, so that it is not pulled downwards during extension.
Then all attendants go to their respective parts, and the doctor equalizes the reduction with his hand.
If it therefore obeys for the reversal, it will be good.
And if not, place it on the table and press it equally, as we said in the case of a hump. The sick person should lie on his healthy side.
When there is a dislocation in front, then it is necessary to quickly extend the sick person's leg. And according to this same narrative on a bed, the doctor should place the palm of his right hand on the sick person's groin. Then bend him with your other hand, and with this bending, place him extended towards the lower parts at the knee joint.
If the dislocation is towards the back: then it is not necessary to extend the sick person towards the lower parts and lift him off the ground. Instead, he should be placed on a hard surface, as is also necessary if his hip joint is dislocated towards the outside, according to what we said about his lying on two hips: namely, on his face and with his legs extended: according to what we said recently.
It is necessary to apply compression with a board also at the place from which the joint departs, when reduction is completed as required.
And the sign of its reduction is not hidden from you: and it is to extend the two legs of the sick person: and when you see them level, and the sick person tightens his leg, and expands himself without support: then you will know that the limb has returned, as required: then join the hip bones, and apply a plaster, and tighten with bandages, that is, a roller: thus, so that the hip joint is not moved to any part: and let the sick person rest quietly for three or four days.
Then loosen the ligament, remove the plaster, and compare the leg with the other: then if you see them level in position: then you will know that the dislocation is already fixed: so release the sick person to walk.
And if you see something softening: then return, and apply a plaster, and bind it according to your first binding: and leave it for three days.
Then loosen that [part] and make it slow in walking on either side for several days, until it recovers well.
Chapter on Dislocations of the Knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways. It [the knee] is dislocated externally, internally, and downwards (backward).
And it is not dislocated anteriorly.
The sign of its [the knee's] dislocation is that you instruct the weak to apply their leg to their hip. If it does not stick with the hip: then know, that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all the ways of its [the knee's] dislocation is that you make the weak sit upright, so that he can now extend his leg over it and let his back touch his face; and put his foot between your feet.
Then make the [knee's] bones adhere with your hands, and place them straight across the [knee] between your fingers. Then apply both sides of the knee with the palm of your hand firmly. And another servant extends his [the patient's] foot until the knee returns to its place.
The sign of its return is that the leg sticks with the hip gently, not forcefully.
Then bandage [the knee] and let the leg stick with the hip. Then bind it together with a plaster for three or four days. Then loosen it.
And the weak one should not walk for some days until he is healed.
If [the knee] refuses to return, then administer a powerful extension with ligaments, the treatment of which has been described earlier for an ankle, until it returns.
Chapter on the Treatment of Ankle Dislocation.
Heel is sometimes moved with slight removal, and at other times dislocated according to its full extent. Its dislocation is either internal or external.
The sign of its dislocation is that you see the heel swollen, projecting towards the part to which it has been dislocated.
The treatment of its removal is easy; it consists in bringing it back easily with the hands and equalizing it until it returns to its place.
